What Is Kalam?
beginner
3 min
The Absolute Definitions of Qidam and Huduth
Sanusi gives two absolute definitions of qidam and huduth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two absolute definitions of qidam and huduth: not being preceded by another (general) and not being preceded by non-existence (conventional).

Core Idea
Qidam and huduth are defined by precedence: absolute or specified to non-existence.

Sanusi opens the first topic by presenting the foundational definitions of qidam and huduth. These two concepts are central to Islamic theology, as they describe the relationship of things to time and existence. The author first gives a general definition, then refines it to the conventional one used by the majority.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first few sentences of the Arabic text where Sanusi presents the two definitions. Note that he introduces parentheses and numbering.
The editorial numbers (1-4) are markers for commentary, not part of the original text.
2
Identify the two definitions
Look for the phrase 'قد يراد بهما' (they are intended as) and then find the two specifications: first without specifying the 'other', then specifying it as non-existence.
The first is more general; the second is the conventional meaning (المتعارف).
3
Understand the conventional meaning
Note that when 'other' is specified as non-existence, qidam means not preceded by non-existence, and huduth means preceded by non-existence. This is the common understanding.
This distinction is crucial for later discussions about the universe's creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول في تفسيرهما وأقسامهما وما يتعلق بذلك
(المبحث الأول : (۱) قد يراد بهما(۲) عدم المسبوقيـة بـالـغيـر والمسبوقية (۳) بــه (٤) ، وقد يخص الغير بالعدم وهو المتعارف، وقد يقالان باعتبار تفاوت ما مضى من زمان الوجود زيادة ونقصا، فالقدم الذاتي أخص من الزماني، وهو من الإضافي والحدوث بالعكس .
والمتصف بهما حقيقة هو الوجود، وأما الموجود فباعتباره، وقد يتصف بهما العدم، فيقال للعدم الغير المسبوق بالوجود قديم، وللمسبوق حادث، ثم كل من القدم والحدوث قد يوجد حقيقياً، وقد يوجد (ه) إضافياً.
أما الحقيقي فقد يراد بالقدم عدم المسبوقية بالغير، وبالحدوث المسبوقية به، ويسمى ذاتياً، وقد يخص الغير بالعدم، فيراد بالقدم عدم المسبوقية بالعدم، وبالحدوث المسبوقية به، وهو معنى الخروج من العدم إلى الوجود، زمانياً، وهذا (٦) هو المتعارف عند الجمهور، وأما الإضافي، فيراد بالقدم كون ما مضى من زمان وجود الشيء أكثر، وبالحدوث كونه أقل.
ويسمى
The first topic: on its interpretation, its divisions, and what pertains to that. (The first topic:) (1) They are intended (2) as the state of not being preceded by another and the state of being preceded (3) by it (4), and sometimes 'another' is specified as nonexistence, which is the conventional meaning, and sometimes they are stated in consideration of the variation of what has passed of the time of existence, increase and decrease. Thus essential preeternity (al-qidam al-dhati) is more specific than temporal preeternity (al-zamani), and it is relative (idafi), and origination (al-huduth) is the opposite. That which is truly described by them is existence (al-wujud); as for the existent (al-mawjud), it is by virtue of it. And sometimes non-existence (al-‘adam) is described by them, so that non-existence not preceded by existence is called qadim, and that which is preceded is called hadith. Then each of al-qidam and al-huduth may be found as real (haqiqi) and may be found (5) as relative (idafi). As for the real, qidam may be intended as the state of not being preceded by another, and huduth as the state of being preceded by it, and it is called essential (dhati). And sometimes 'another' is specified as non-existence, so that qidam is intended as the state of not being preceded by non-existence, and huduth as the state of being preceded by it, and this is the meaning of emerging from non-existence into existence, temporally; and this (6) is the conventional meaning according to the majority. As for the relative, qidam is intended as that what has passed of the time of the thing's existence is more, and huduth as that it is less. And it is called...
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the two key kalam terms: qidam (preeternity/eternity a parte ante) and huduth (origination/temporal creation). The author begins by stating that these terms can be defined in two ways: (1) as 'not being preceded by another' and 'being preceded by another', where 'another' can be unspecified or specifically identified as non-existence (the conventional meaning), and (2) in a relative sense based on the comparative length of time elapsed. The author then notes that the essential (dhati) qidam is more specific than the temporal (zamani) qidam, and that the essential qidam is actually relative (idafi) — a subcategory. The attribution of these terms is discussed: truly, they apply to existence itself; an existent is described derivatively. Interestingly, they can also apply to non-existence: a non-existence not preceded by existence is called qadim (like the non-existence of a thing before its creation?), and one preceded by existence is called hadith (like the non-existence after annihilation?). Next, the passage distinguishes between real (haqiqi) and relative (idafi) forms. The real qidam and huduth are further subdivided: one interpretation defines qidam as not being preceded by another (anything at all) and huduth as being preceded by another — this is called essential (dhati). Another interpretation specifies 'another' as non-existence, so qidam means not being preceded by non-existence (i.e., existence from eternity) and huduth means being preceded by non-existence (i.e., coming into existence after non-existence) — this is the temporal meaning, which is the conventional understanding among the majority of scholars. Relative qidam and huduth depend on comparison: if the past duration of a thing's existence is longer than another's, it is called qadim in a relative sense; if shorter, it is hadith. The passage ends abruptly with 'and it is called...' indicating continuation.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
حدوث (huduth)
عدم (adam)
مسبوقية (sabqiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the conventional definition of qidam?
A) Not being preceded by anything at all
B) Not being preceded by non-existence
C) Having the longest possible past existence
D) Being free from any cause

Key Takeaway
Qidam and huduth are defined by precedence; the conventional meaning specifies precedence relative to non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that Allah is qadim (not preceded by non-existence) while you are hadith (preceded by non-existence). Does this truth humble you? In what specific ways does your daily life reflect your createdness and dependence on the Eternal? Will you change anything today to live more consciously as a huduth being?
?
The conventional definition of huduth specifies 'preceded by non-existence' rather than just 'preceded by something else'. Why do you think the scholars emphasized non-existence? Consider your own beginning: you were nothing before Allah created you. How does this personal reality affect your gratitude and submission to Him right now?

intermediate

The Relative Definition and Subjects of Qidam and Huduth
Sanusi gives the relative definition and explains what can be described by these terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that qidam and huduth can also be defined relatively based on the length of past time, and that they apply to existence and nonexistence.

Core Idea
Qidam and huduth are relative: longer past means older; they also apply to nonexistence.

After the absolute definitions, Sanusi introduces another way to understand qidam and huduth: relatively, by comparing how much time has passed for a thing's existence. He then discusses what can be described by these terms, including existence itself and even non-existence in a certain sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
From 'وقد يقالان باعتبار' up to 'ويسمى' (before the cut). Note the relative definition and then the discussion of subject.
2
Understand the relative definition
The passage says qidam and huduth can be said based on the variation of what has passed of existence time: if more, it's qadim; if less, it's hadith.
This is a comparative sense, not absolute.
3
Identify the subjects
Note that truly (haqiqatan) these terms apply to existence itself, but they can also be applied to an existent (mawjud) derivatively, and even to non-existence (adam) in some contexts.
The example: non-existence not preceded by existence is called qadim, and one preceded by existence is called hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول في تفسيرهما وأقسامهما وما يتعلق بذلك
(المبحث الأول : (۱) قد يراد بهما(۲) عدم المسبوقيـة بـالـغيـر والمسبوقية (۳) بــه (٤) ، وقد يخص الغير بالعدم وهو المتعارف، وقد يقالان باعتبار تفاوت ما مضى من زمان الوجود زيادة ونقصا، فالقدم الذاتي أخص من الزماني، وهو من الإضافي والحدوث بالعكس .
والمتصف بهما حقيقة هو الوجود، وأما الموجود فباعتباره، وقد يتصف بهما العدم، فيقال للعدم الغير المسبوق بالوجود قديم، وللمسبوق حادث، ثم كل من القدم والحدوث قد يوجد حقيقياً، وقد يوجد (ه) إضافياً.
أما الحقيقي فقد يراد بالقدم عدم المسبوقية بالغير، وبالحدوث المسبوقية به، ويسمى ذاتياً، وقد يخص الغير بالعدم، فيراد بالقدم عدم المسبوقية بالعدم، وبالحدوث المسبوقية به، وهو معنى الخروج من العدم إلى الوجود، زمانياً، وهذا (٦) هو المتعارف عند الجمهور، وأما الإضافي، فيراد بالقدم كون ما مضى من زمان وجود الشيء أكثر، وبالحدوث كونه أقل.
ويسمى
The first topic: on its interpretation, its divisions, and what pertains to that. (The first topic:) (1) They are intended (2) as the state of not being preceded by another and the state of being preceded (3) by it (4), and sometimes 'another' is specified as nonexistence, which is the conventional meaning, and sometimes they are stated in consideration of the variation of what has passed of the time of existence, increase and decrease. Thus essential preeternity (al-qidam al-dhati) is more specific than temporal preeternity (al-zamani), and it is relative (idafi), and origination (al-huduth) is the opposite. That which is truly described by them is existence (al-wujud); as for the existent (al-mawjud), it is by virtue of it. And sometimes non-existence (al-‘adam) is described by them, so that non-existence not preceded by existence is called qadim, and that which is preceded is called hadith. Then each of al-qidam and al-huduth may be found as real (haqiqi) and may be found (5) as relative (idafi). As for the real, qidam may be intended as the state of not being preceded by another, and huduth as the state of being preceded by it, and it is called essential (dhati). And sometimes 'another' is specified as non-existence, so that qidam is intended as the state of not being preceded by non-existence, and huduth as the state of being preceded by it, and this is the meaning of emerging from non-existence into existence, temporally; and this (6) is the conventional meaning according to the majority. As for the relative, qidam is intended as that what has passed of the time of the thing's existence is more, and huduth as that it is less. And it is called...
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the two key kalam terms: qidam (preeternity/eternity a parte ante) and huduth (origination/temporal creation). The author begins by stating that these terms can be defined in two ways: (1) as 'not being preceded by another' and 'being preceded by another', where 'another' can be unspecified or specifically identified as non-existence (the conventional meaning), and (2) in a relative sense based on the comparative length of time elapsed. The author then notes that the essential (dhati) qidam is more specific than the temporal (zamani) qidam, and that the essential qidam is actually relative (idafi) — a subcategory. The attribution of these terms is discussed: truly, they apply to existence itself; an existent is described derivatively. Interestingly, they can also apply to non-existence: a non-existence not preceded by existence is called qadim (like the non-existence of a thing before its creation?), and one preceded by existence is called hadith (like the non-existence after annihilation?). Next, the passage distinguishes between real (haqiqi) and relative (idafi) forms. The real qidam and huduth are further subdivided: one interpretation defines qidam as not being preceded by another (anything at all) and huduth as being preceded by another — this is called essential (dhati). Another interpretation specifies 'another' as non-existence, so qidam means not being preceded by non-existence (i.e., existence from eternity) and huduth means being preceded by non-existence (i.e., coming into existence after non-existence) — this is the temporal meaning, which is the conventional understanding among the majority of scholars. Relative qidam and huduth depend on comparison: if the past duration of a thing's existence is longer than another's, it is called qadim in a relative sense; if shorter, it is hadith. The passage ends abruptly with 'and it is called...' indicating continuation.

Key Terms
إضافي (idafi)
وجود (wujud)
موجود (mawjud)
عدم (adam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think qidam and huduth only apply to existence, not to non-existence.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that non-existence can be described by these terms: a non-existence not preceded by existence is qadim, and one preceded by existence is hadith.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the true subject of qidam and huduth?
A) The existent (mawjud)
B) Existence (wujud)
C) Non-existence (adam)
D) Time (zaman)

Key Takeaway
Qidam and huduth can be relative based on time, and they primarily describe existence, but can also describe non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You will one day be non-existent. How does the lesson's classification of non-existence as either qadim (not preceded by existence) or hadith (preceded by existence) affect your perspective on your own mortality? Will your non-existence be qadim or hadith? What does that mean for your current actions?
?
The passage teaches that qidam and huduth are relative based on time. In your life, are there things you treat as permanent (qadim) that are actually relative? How might this realization challenge you to reassess your priorities and focus on what is truly absolute?
?
If even non-existence can be described as qadim or hadith, what does that imply about the nature of time and your relationship with the Eternal (Al-Qadim)? How does this understanding deepen your sense of accountability before Allah?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
Real vs. Relative: Essential and Temporal Qidam and Huduth
Sanusi divides qidam and huduth into real and relative, and subdivides real into essential and temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will distinguish real (haqiqi) from relative (idafi) qidam and huduth, and understand the two subtypes of real: essential (dhati) and temporal (zamani).

Core Idea
Real qidam is either essential (not preceded by anything) or temporal (not preceded by non-existence); relative is comparative.

After establishing definitions, Sanusi deepens the analysis by dividing qidam and huduth into real and relative. The real is further subdivided: essential (dhati) and temporal (zamani), where the temporal meaning is the conventional one among the majority. Understanding this taxonomy is key to grasping later arguments about God's attributes and the world's creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the third part of the passage
From 'أما الحقيقي' to the end (ويسمى). Focus on the two interpretations of the real and the definition of relative.
2
Distinguish real vs relative
Real (haqiqi) qidam/huduth are based on absolute precedence; relative (idafi) are based on comparison of time passed.
3
Identify the two types of real
Real qidam can mean not being preceded by another (anything) – this is essential (dhati). Or it can mean not being preceded by non-existence – this is temporal (zamani), the conventional meaning.
Note that essential is more specific than temporal, and essential is actually relative? (The passage says 'essential is more specific than temporal, and it is relative' – this may be clarified elsewhere.)
4
Define relative qidam and huduth
Relative: if the past duration of existence is longer, it is qadim; if shorter, it is hadith. Also called 'idafi'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول في تفسيرهما وأقسامهما وما يتعلق بذلك
(المبحث الأول : (۱) قد يراد بهما(۲) عدم المسبوقيـة بـالـغيـر والمسبوقية (۳) بــه (٤) ، وقد يخص الغير بالعدم وهو المتعارف، وقد يقالان باعتبار تفاوت ما مضى من زمان الوجود زيادة ونقصا، فالقدم الذاتي أخص من الزماني، وهو من الإضافي والحدوث بالعكس .
والمتصف بهما حقيقة هو الوجود، وأما الموجود فباعتباره، وقد يتصف بهما العدم، فيقال للعدم الغير المسبوق بالوجود قديم، وللمسبوق حادث، ثم كل من القدم والحدوث قد يوجد حقيقياً، وقد يوجد (ه) إضافياً.
أما الحقيقي فقد يراد بالقدم عدم المسبوقية بالغير، وبالحدوث المسبوقية به، ويسمى ذاتياً، وقد يخص الغير بالعدم، فيراد بالقدم عدم المسبوقية بالعدم، وبالحدوث المسبوقية به، وهو معنى الخروج من العدم إلى الوجود، زمانياً، وهذا (٦) هو المتعارف عند الجمهور، وأما الإضافي، فيراد بالقدم كون ما مضى من زمان وجود الشيء أكثر، وبالحدوث كونه أقل.
ويسمى
The first topic: on its interpretation, its divisions, and what pertains to that. (The first topic:) (1) They are intended (2) as the state of not being preceded by another and the state of being preceded (3) by it (4), and sometimes 'another' is specified as nonexistence, which is the conventional meaning, and sometimes they are stated in consideration of the variation of what has passed of the time of existence, increase and decrease. Thus essential preeternity (al-qidam al-dhati) is more specific than temporal preeternity (al-zamani), and it is relative (idafi), and origination (al-huduth) is the opposite. That which is truly described by them is existence (al-wujud); as for the existent (al-mawjud), it is by virtue of it. And sometimes non-existence (al-‘adam) is described by them, so that non-existence not preceded by existence is called qadim, and that which is preceded is called hadith. Then each of al-qidam and al-huduth may be found as real (haqiqi) and may be found (5) as relative (idafi). As for the real, qidam may be intended as the state of not being preceded by another, and huduth as the state of being preceded by it, and it is called essential (dhati). And sometimes 'another' is specified as non-existence, so that qidam is intended as the state of not being preceded by non-existence, and huduth as the state of being preceded by it, and this is the meaning of emerging from non-existence into existence, temporally; and this (6) is the conventional meaning according to the majority. As for the relative, qidam is intended as that what has passed of the time of the thing's existence is more, and huduth as that it is less. And it is called...

Commentary

The author then notes that the essential (dhati) qidam is more specific than the temporal (zamani) qidam, and that the essential qidam is actually relative (idafi) — a subcategory. The attribution of these terms is discussed: truly, they apply to existence itself; an existent is described derivatively. Interestingly, they can also apply to non-existence: a non-existence not preceded by existence is called qadim (like the non-existence of a thing before its creation?), and one preceded by existence is called hadith (like the non-existence after annihilation?). Next, the passage distinguishes between real (haqiqi) and relative (idafi) forms. The real qidam and huduth are further subdivided: one interpretation defines qidam as not being preceded by another (anything at all) and huduth as being preceded by another — this is called essential (dhati). The passage ends abruptly with 'and it is called...' indicating continuation.

إضافي (idafi)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'real' qidam always means 'not preceded by non-existence' (temporal), but the passage shows there is also a more essential meaning: not being preceded by anything.
Correction: The real qidam has two forms: essential (dhati) which is absolute, and temporal (zamani) which is conventional.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which meaning of qidam is the conventional one according to the majority?
A) Not being preceded by another
B) Not being preceded by non-existence
C) Having a longer past existence compared to something else
D) Being prior in essence

Key Takeaway
Real qidam and huduth are either essential (absolute) or temporal (relative to non-existence); relative qidam is comparative in time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that temporal qidam means never having been preceded by non-existence—only Allah possesses this. Do you truly settle in your heart that nothing else shares this attribute? When you say 'Allah is eternal,' do your actions and thoughts reflect that you believe He alone has no beginning? What would change in your daily life if you felt the weight of this truth right now?
?
Your own existence is temporal huduth: you were once non-existent, then brought into being by Allah. How does this reality make you feel about your dependency on Him? Does it humble you or make you uncomfortable? Pause and consider: if you truly believed you were nothing without Him, would your current worries and ambitions look different? Commit to one practical way to live this dependency today.
?
The relative qidam and huduth compare durations of existence—some things are older than others. When you compare your brief life to the timelessness of Allah, what emotion stirs in you? Does it inspire awe, urgency, or indifference? How should this perspective shape what you prioritize before you return to the One who has always existed?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
5 min
Types of Pre-eternity and Temporal Origination
Defines the three types of qidam and three types of huduth with logical relationships.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of pre-eternity (qidam) and three types of temporal origination (huduth) and the logical relationships between them.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity and temporal origination each have three levels, with essential being the most specific for qidam.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid introduces a foundational classification in Islamic theology: the types of pre-eternity (qidam) and temporal origination (huduth). Understanding these categories is essential for distinguishing God's attributes from created things. The author uses logical relationships of specificity to show the hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses logical terms like 'أخص من' (more specific than).
2
Identify the three types of qidam
Find the three types: essential (dhati), temporal (zamani), and relational (idafi). Note how the author defines each and their logical order.
Essential is the most specific; it implies the others but not vice versa.
3
Identify the three types of huduth
Find the three types: relational (idafi), temporal (zamani), and essential (dhati). Note the reverse hierarchy compared to qidam.
Relational is the most specific for huduth.
4
Reflect on the logical relationships
Ask yourself why essential qidam is most specific and relational huduth is most specific. Use the examples given (attributes of God, father and son) to understand.
This connects to the learning objective about logical relationships.

فالقدم الذاتي أخص من الزماني والزماني من الإضافي، بمعنى أن كل ما ليس مسبوقا بالغير أصلاً ليس مسبوقاً بالعدم ولا عكس، كما في صفات الواجب وكل ما ليس مسبوقاً بالعدم، فما مضى من زمان وجوده يكون أكثر بالنسبة إلى ما حدث بعده ولا عكس كالأب فإنه أقدم من الابن، وليس قديماً بالزمان، والحدوث الإضافي أخص من الزماني، والزماني من الذاتي، بمعنى أن كل ما يكون زمان وجوده الماضي أقل فهو (۱) مسبوق بالعدم، ولا عكس، وكل ما هو مسبوق بالعدم، فهو مسبوق بالغير ولا عكس .
Essential pre-eternity is more specific than temporal pre-eternity, and temporal pre-eternity is more specific than relational pre-eternity, meaning that everything that is not preceded by another [cause] at all is not preceded by non-existence, and not vice versa, as in the attributes of the Necessary Being. And everything that is not preceded by non-existence, then the past time of its existence is greater relative to what occurs after it, and not vice versa, like the father, for he is more ancient than the son, but not pre-eternal in time. And relational temporal origination is more specific than temporal [origination], and temporal [origination] is more specific than essential [origination], meaning that everything for which the past time of its existence is less is (1) preceded by non-existence, and not vice versa, and everything that is preceded by non-existence is preceded by another [cause], and not vice versa.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the concepts of pre-eternity (qidam) and temporal origination (huduth) into three types each: essential (dhati), temporal (zamani), and relational (idafi). It begins by stating the logical relationships: essential pre-eternity is more specific than temporal, which is more specific than relational. The explanation follows: whatever is not preceded by any other (i.e., independent of any cause) is also not preceded by non-existence, meaning it necessarily exists without beginning; this applies to God's attributes. Conversely, whatever is preceded by non-existence has a finite past relative to later things, like a father being older than his son but not temporally pre-eternal. For huduth, relational origination is most specific, then temporal, then essential. The passage clarifies that anything with a shorter past existence is temporally originated (preceded by non-existence), and anything preceded by non-existence is also preceded by another cause (since non-existence implies a cause for existence). The author uses logical relationships of 'special' and 'general' to show the hierarchy of these concepts.

Key Terms

القدم الزماني
القدم الإضافي
الحدوث الذاتي
الحدوث الزماني
الحدوث الإضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that temporal pre-eternity (e.g., the universe having no beginning) is the same as essential pre-eternity (not caused by anything).
Correction: Essential pre-eternity is more specific: it implies not being preceded by any cause at all, whereas temporal pre-eternity only implies no beginning in time. God alone has essential pre-eternity.

Checkpoint
Which type of pre-eternity is the most specific according to the passage?
A) Temporal pre-eternity (al-qidam al-zamani)
B) Essential pre-eternity (al-qidam al-dhati)
C) Relational pre-eternity (al-qidam al-idafi)
D) All are equally specific

Key Takeaway
The three types of pre-eternity and temporal origination form a hierarchy based on logical specificity, with essential pre-eternity and relational origination being the most specific in their respective categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that God is essentially pre-eternal, not preceded by any cause. Does your daily awareness of His eternal nature actually change how you prioritize His commands over your fleeting desires? Be honest: when was the last time you delayed a prayer or ignored a prohibition because something worldly seemed more urgent? How does this lesson's distinction between essential and temporal pre-eternity expose the fallacy in that choice?
?
The lesson says that temporal pre-eternity (not being preceded by non-existence) does not necessarily imply essential pre-eternity. Your own soul has a beginning; it is temporally originated. Does that humble you? In what specific area of your life have you been acting as if your own plans or opinions are eternal, as if they have no cause or beginning?
?
Relational huduth means your existence is shorter than others. You will die, and your time will cease. If you truly internalized that you are only a few decades old compared to the earth's history, what would you change about how you spend your time today? Write down one concrete action you will take right now because of this truth.

Definition of Matter and Duration in the Philosophers' Claim
Taftazani defines matter/duration and clarifies pre-eternity claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of the philosophers' claim that matter and time are pre-eternal, and how Taftazani clarifies it against two common misinterpretations.

Core Idea
The philosophers' 'eternal matter' does not mean individual substrates or infinite chains, but a single simple substrate for all generated things.

Taftazani here unpacks the key terms of the philosophers' argument for the world's contingency. He defines what they mean by 'matter' and 'duration', then carefully explains their assertion that matter and time are pre-eternal. The passage shows the importance of precise definitions in theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's wording sink in.
Notice the technical terms: مادة, مدة, هيولا, etc.
2
Identify the three definitions of matter
Taftazani defines matter for three types of contingent: accident, form, soul. Find each definition and note the Arabic term used for each (subject, hyle, what it pertains to).
3
Understand the claim about pre-eternity
Taftazani first mentions two rejected meanings (individual substrates eternal, infinite regress) and then states the correct meaning (a single simple eternal matter). List these three parts.
This is the core of the philosophical position.
4
Reflect on why the clarification matters
Consider why rejecting infinite regress is important. Ask: how does this definition avoid a logical absurdity?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زعمت الفلاسفة أن كل حادث مسبوق بمادة ومدة. أي موجود بعدم العدم مسبوق بمادة ومدة. وعنوا بالمادة ما يكون موضوعاً للحادث إن كان عرضاً، أو هيولا إن كان صورة، أو متعلقة إن كان نفساً، وبالمدة الزمان. وبنوا على ذلك، قدم المادة والزمان، لا بمعنى أن محل هذا السواد، ومحل هذه النفس مثلاً قديم لظهور إستحالته، ولا بمعنى أن قبل كل مادة مادة لا إلى بداية كما في الحركة والزمان (لأنه يستلزم اجتماع المواد الغير متناهية في الوجود ضرورة أن كلاً منها جزء ما تركبت عنه وهو محال)، بل بمعنى أنه لا بد أن يكون للمركب مادة بسيطة قديمة، هي الحاصل للصور والأعراض الحادثة، إذ لو كانت حادثة لكانت لها مادة أخرى وتسلسل.
The philosophers claimed that every contingent event is preceded by matter and duration. That is, an existence after non-existence preceded by matter and duration. They meant by matter that which is the subject of the contingency if it is an accident, or hyle if it is a form, or what it pertains to if it is a soul; and by duration, time. They built upon that the pre-eternity of matter and time — not meaning that the substratum of this blackness and the substratum of this soul, for example, are eternal, due to the manifest impossibility of that; nor meaning that every matter is preceded by another matter without beginning, as in motion and time (because that entails an infinite collection of matters existing, since each one is part of what is composed of them, which is impossible), but rather meaning that it is necessary for the composite to have a simple eternal matter, which is the substrate for the forms and accidents that come into being, for if they were contingent, they would need another matter, leading to infinite regress.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage defines the philosophers' key terms for their argument about the contingency of the world. It begins by stating the claim that every contingent event (hadith) is preceded by matter (madda) and duration (mudda), explaining that 'preceded by matter and duration' means 'existing after non-existence while having matter and duration'. It then defines matter according to the type of contingent: for an accident, matter is its subject; for a substantial form, matter is hyle (prime matter); for a soul, matter is that to which it relates. Duration is simply time. The passage then clarifies the exact meaning of the philosophers' conclusion that matter and time are pre-eternal (qadim). It rejects two misinterpretations: first, that individual substrata (e.g., the substrate of this particular blackness) are eternal—this is obviously false. Second, that there is an infinite chain of matter without a beginning, as in motion and time—this would entail an actual infinite collection of matters, which is impossible. Instead, the correct meaning is that every composite must have a simple, eternal matter that underlies all forms and accidents; otherwise, if that matter itself were contingent, it would require another matter, leading to an infinite regress.

Key Terms

مادة
مدة
هيولا
عرض
صورة
نفس
زمان
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pre-eternity of matter means that every particular substrate (e.g., the bearer of blackness) is eternal, or that there is an infinite chain of matters without beginning.
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that pre-eternity refers to a single simple eternal matter underlying all generated things, not eternal individual substrates or an infinite regress.

Checkpoint

A) Every particular substrate, like the bearer of blackness, is eternal.
B) There is an infinite chain of matter without a beginning, like motion and time.
C) Every composite must have a simple eternal matter that underlies forms and accidents.
D) Matter and time are co-eternal with God.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' claim of eternal matter is specifically about a simple, primary substratum that underlies all generated forms and accidents, not about eternal individuals or infinite chains.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Taftazani explains what the philosophers did and did not mean by eternal matter. The philosophers argued for an eternal prime matter to explain the generation of things without invoking God. Muslim theologians rejected this based on revelation. What would it cost you to align your reasoning with revelation instead?

Unity and Multiplicity as Rational Considerations
Classification of unity/multiplicity as rational considerations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that unity and multiplicity are purely rational concepts with no external existence, and that their conception is intuitive, requiring only verbal definition.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity exist only in the mind, not in external reality.

Al-Taftazani opens the discussion on unity and multiplicity by establishing that they are not real properties of objects but mental considerations. This sets the stage for understanding how theologians reason about these concepts. The passage emphasizes their intuitive nature and explains why they are only defined verbally.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification
Read the passage and note that unity and multiplicity are classified as 'rational considerations' (i'tibārāt 'aqliyya). This means they are mental constructs, not real attributes.
Contrast with real properties like color or size.
2
Grasp the intuitive nature
Notice that their conception is described as intuitive (badīhī), meaning they are known without training. Reflect on why this makes them independent of formal definition.
This relates to the concept of 'badīhī' in Islamic epistemology.
3
Identify the verbal definitions
The passage states that they are only known verbally (lafẓan). Pay attention to the examples given: unity as absence of division, multiplicity as division.
4
Connect to earlier topics
Al-Taftazani compares this to the discussion of necessity and contingency. Recall that those were also rational considerations. This reinforces the framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَبْحَثُ الْأَوَّلُ الْوَحْدَةُ وَالْكَثْرَةُ مِنَ الِاعْتِبَارَاتِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ. قَالَ: الْمَنْهَجُ الرَّابِعُ فِي الْوَحْدَةِ وَالْكَثْرَةِ، وَفِيهِ مَبَاحِثُ: الْمَبْحَثُ الْأَوَّلُ أَنَّهُمَا مِنَ الِاعْتِبَارَاتِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ الْغَنِيَّةِ عَنِ التَّعْرِيفِ، إِلَّا أَنَّ الْوَحْدَةَ أَعْرَفُ عِنْدَ الْعَقْلِ وَالْكَثْرَةَ عِنْدَ الْخَيَالِ وَلِذَا يَقَعُ كُلٌّ فِي تَفْسِيرِ الْآخَرِ. وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّهُمَا مِنَ الِاعْتِبَارَاتِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ الَّتِي لَا وُجُودَ لَهَا فِي الْأَعْيَانِ. بِمِثْلِ مَا سَبَقَ فِي الْوُجُوبِ وَالْإِمْكَانِ وَإِنْ تَصَوُّرَهُمَا بَدِيهِيٌّ لِحُصُولِهِ لِمَنْ لَمْ يُمارِسْ طَرِيقَ الِاكْتِسَابِ، فَلَا يُعْرَفَانِ إِلَّا لَفْظًا كَمَا يُقَالُ الْوَحْدَةُ عَدَمُ الِانْقِسَامِ، وَالْكَثْرَةُ هُوَ الِانْقِسَامُ، وَقَدْ يُقَالُ الْوَحْدَةُ عَدَمُ الِانْقِسَامِ إِلَى أُمُورٍ مُتَشَابِهَةٍ، وَالْكَثْرَةُ هُوَ الِانْقِسَامُ إِلَيْهَا، وَلَا خَفَاءَ فِي انْتِقَاضِهِمَا طَرْدًا وَعَكْسًا بِالْمُجْتَمَعِ مِنَ الْأُمُورِ الْمُتَخَالِفَةِ، وَأَمَّا مَا يُقَالُ أَنَّ الْوَحْدَةَ عَدَمُ الْكَثْرَةِ وَالْكَثْرَةَ هِيَ الْمُجْتَمِعُ مِنَ الْوَحَدَاتِ فَمَبْنَاهُ عَلَى أَنَّ الْوَحْدَةَ أَعْرَفُ عِنْدَ الْعَقْلِ، وَالْكَثْرَةَ عِنْدَ الْخَيَالِ لِمَا أَنَّ الْوَحْدَةَ مَبْدَأُ الْكَثْرَةِ، وَالْعَقْلُ إِنَّمَا يَعْرِفُ الْمَبْدَأَ قَبْلَ ذِي الْمَبْدَأِ وَالْكَثْرَةَ تَرْتَسِمُ صُوَرُهَا فِي الْخَيَالِ فَيَنْتَزِعُ الْعَقْلُ مِنْهَا أَمْرًا وَاحِدًا، فَيَكُونُ تَفْسِيرُ الْوَحْدَةِ بِالْكثْرَةِ عِنْدَ الْخَيَالِ، وَتَفْسِيرُ الْكَثْرَةِ بِالْوَحْدَةِ عِنْدَ الْعَقْلِ تَفْسِيرًا بِالْأَعْرَافِ لَا بِالْمَارِي فِي الْمَعْرِفَةِ وَالْجَهَالَةِ.
The first investigation: unity and multiplicity are from the rational considerations. He said: The fourth method is on unity and multiplicity, and it contains investigations: the first investigation is that they are from rational considerations that are independent of definition, except that unity is better known to reason and multiplicity to imagination, and therefore each falls into the interpretation of the other. And the truth is that they are from rational considerations that have no existence in external entities. Similar to what preceded in necessity and contingency, even though their conception is intuitive because it occurs to one who has not practiced the path of acquisition, so they are only known verbally, just as it is said: unity is the absence of division, and multiplicity is division; and it is said: unity is the absence of division into similar things, and multiplicity is the division into them. And there is no concealment of their invalidity positively and negatively by the aggregate of different things. And as for what is said that unity is the absence of multiplicity and multiplicity is the aggregate of units, its basis is that unity is better known to reason and multiplicity to imagination, because unity is the principle of multiplicity, and reason only knows the principle before that which has the principle, and multiplicity's images are imprinted in the imagination, then reason extracts from them a single thing. So the interpretation of unity by multiplicity in the imagination, and the interpretation of multiplicity by unity in reason, is an interpretation by convention, not by what imparts knowledge and ignorance.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of unity (al-wahda) and multiplicity (al-kathra) as rational considerations (i'tibārāt 'aqliyya). Al-Taftazani begins by stating that these are concepts that do not require real definition because they are intuitively known, but they differ in their familiarity: unity is better known to reason (al-'aql) while multiplicity is better known to imagination (al-khayāl). This explains why each is used to define the other. He then confirms that they are purely mental considerations with no existence in external reality, like the earlier discussion of necessity and contingency (al-wujūb wa al-imkān). Their conception is intuitive (badīhī), so they are only defined verbally (lafẓan). Two common verbal definitions are given: (1) unity is non-division and multiplicity is division; (2) unity is non-division into similar parts and multiplicity is division into similar parts. He notes that these definitions are not universally valid because they fail when applied to a composite of different things. Another common definition—'unity is the absence of multiplicity, multiplicity is the aggregate of units'—is then evaluated. This definition is based on the epistemological priority: since unity is a principle (mabda') and reason knows the principle before its derivative, unity is better known to reason; since multiplicity is perceived by imagination which forms images, it is better known to imagination. Thus, interpreting unity via multiplicity in imagination and multiplicity via unity in reason is merely conventional (bi al-a'rāf), not a true explanation that imparts knowledge or removes ignorance.

Key Terms
الوحدة (al-wahda)
الكثرة (al-kathra)
الاعتبارات العقلية (al-i'tibārāt al-'aqliyya)
البديهي (al-badīhī)
التعريف اللفظي (al-ta'rīf al-lafẓī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity and multiplicity are real properties of external objects.
Correction: Al-Taftazani explicitly states they are rational considerations with no existence in external entities, similar to necessity and contingency.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity are purely mental concepts, intuitively known, and require only verbal definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How do you know that your understanding of tawḥīd is not just a thought in your mind? What would it take for you to experience tawḥīd as a reality beyond concepts?

?

What practice will you adopt to move from conceptual unity to a deep conviction of God's absolute oneness? How will you remind yourself daily that your mental constructs are not the reality?

Verbal Definitions and the Role of Reason and Imagination
Verbal definitions and epistemological priority of reason/imagination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the common verbal definitions of unity and multiplicity, their limitations, and why unity is better known to reason while multiplicity is better known to imagination.

Core Idea
Reason grasps unity first; imagination grasps multiplicity first.

After establishing that unity and multiplicity are rational considerations, al-Taftazani turns to their verbal definitions. He examines several common definitions, critiques their validity, and explains the epistemological basis for defining one in terms of the other. This lesson explores these definitions and the roles of reason and imagination.

Guided Path
1
Examine the verbal definitions
Read the definitions provided: unity as absence of division, multiplicity as division; also unity as absence of division into similar parts. Notice the author's critique of these definitions.
2
Understand the critique
Al-Taftazani points out that these definitions fail when applied to a composite of different things. Reflect on why this is a problem.
The term 'connote and denote' (ṭardan wa 'aksan) refers to logical validity.
3
Learn the epistemological priority
The author explains that unity is better known to reason because it is a principle, and reason knows principles first. Multiplicity is better known to imagination because images are imprinted there.
4
Apply the conventional interpretation
The passage concludes that interpreting unity via multiplicity in imagination and vice versa is merely conventional, not a true explanation.

الْمَبْحَثُ الْأَوَّلُ الْوَحْدَةُ وَالْكَثْرَةُ مِنَ الِاعْتِبَارَاتِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ. قَالَ: الْمَنْهَجُ الرَّابِعُ فِي الْوَحْدَةِ وَالْكَثْرَةِ، وَفِيهِ مَبَاحِثُ: الْمَبْحَثُ الْأَوَّلُ أَنَّهُمَا مِنَ الِاعْتِبَارَاتِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ الْغَنِيَّةِ عَنِ التَّعْرِيفِ، إِلَّا أَنَّ الْوَحْدَةَ أَعْرَفُ عِنْدَ الْعَقْلِ وَالْكَثْرَةَ عِنْدَ الْخَيَالِ وَلِذَا يَقَعُ كُلٌّ فِي تَفْسِيرِ الْآخَرِ. وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّهُمَا مِنَ الِاعْتِبَارَاتِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ الَّتِي لَا وُجُودَ لَهَا فِي الْأَعْيَانِ. بِمِثْلِ مَا سَبَقَ فِي الْوُجُوبِ وَالْإِمْكَانِ وَإِنْ تَصَوُّرَهُمَا بَدِيهِيٌّ لِحُصُولِهِ لِمَنْ لَمْ يُمارِسْ طَرِيقَ الِاكْتِسَابِ، فَلَا يُعْرَفَانِ إِلَّا لَفْظًا كَمَا يُقَالُ الْوَحْدَةُ عَدَمُ الِانْقِسَامِ، وَالْكَثْرَةُ هُوَ الِانْقِسَامُ، وَقَدْ يُقَالُ الْوَحْدَةُ عَدَمُ الِانْقِسَامِ إِلَى أُمُورٍ مُتَشَابِهَةٍ، وَالْكَثْرَةُ هُوَ الِانْقِسَامُ إِلَيْهَا، وَلَا خَفَاءَ فِي انْتِقَاضِهِمَا طَرْدًا وَعَكْسًا بِالْمُجْتَمَعِ مِنَ الْأُمُورِ الْمُتَخَالِفَةِ، وَأَمَّا مَا يُقَالُ أَنَّ الْوَحْدَةَ عَدَمُ الْكَثْرَةِ وَالْكَثْرَةَ هِيَ الْمُجْتَمِعُ مِنَ الْوَحَدَاتِ فَمَبْنَاهُ عَلَى أَنَّ الْوَحْدَةَ أَعْرَفُ عِنْدَ الْعَقْلِ، وَالْكَثْرَةَ عِنْدَ الْخَيَالِ لِمَا أَنَّ الْوَحْدَةَ مَبْدَأُ الْكَثْرَةِ، وَالْعَقْلُ إِنَّمَا يَعْرِفُ الْمَبْدَأَ قَبْلَ ذِي الْمَبْدَأِ وَالْكَثْرَةَ تَرْتَسِمُ صُوَرُهَا فِي الْخَيَالِ فَيَنْتَزِعُ الْعَقْلُ مِنْهَا أَمْرًا وَاحِدًا، فَيَكُونُ تَفْسِيرُ الْوَحْدَةِ بِالْكثْرَةِ عِنْدَ الْخَيَالِ، وَتَفْسِيرُ الْكَثْرَةِ بِالْوَحْدَةِ عِنْدَ الْعَقْلِ تَفْسِيرًا بِالْأَعْرَافِ لَا بِالْمَارِي فِي الْمَعْرِفَةِ وَالْجَهَالَةِ.
The first investigation: unity and multiplicity are from the rational considerations. He said: The fourth method is on unity and multiplicity, and it contains investigations: the first investigation is that they are from rational considerations that are independent of definition, except that unity is better known to reason and multiplicity to imagination, and therefore each falls into the interpretation of the other. And the truth is that they are from rational considerations that have no existence in external entities. Similar to what preceded in necessity and contingency, even though their conception is intuitive because it occurs to one who has not practiced the path of acquisition, so they are only known verbally, just as it is said: unity is the absence of division, and multiplicity is division; and it is said: unity is the absence of division into similar things, and multiplicity is the division into them. And there is no concealment of their invalidity positively and negatively by the aggregate of different things. And as for what is said that unity is the absence of multiplicity and multiplicity is the aggregate of units, its basis is that unity is better known to reason and multiplicity to imagination, because unity is the principle of multiplicity, and reason only knows the principle before that which has the principle, and multiplicity's images are imprinted in the imagination, then reason extracts from them a single thing. So the interpretation of unity by multiplicity in the imagination, and the interpretation of multiplicity by unity in reason, is an interpretation by convention, not by what imparts knowledge and ignorance.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of unity (al-wahda) and multiplicity (al-kathra) as rational considerations (i'tibārāt 'aqliyya). Al-Taftazani begins by stating that these are concepts that do not require real definition because they are intuitively known, but they differ in their familiarity: unity is better known to reason (al-'aql) while multiplicity is better known to imagination (al-khayāl). This explains why each is used to define the other. He then confirms that they are purely mental considerations with no existence in external reality, like the earlier discussion of necessity and contingency (al-wujūb wa al-imkān). Their conception is intuitive (badīhī), so they are only defined verbally (lafẓan). Two common verbal definitions are given: (1) unity is non-division and multiplicity is division; (2) unity is non-division into similar parts and multiplicity is division into similar parts. He notes that these definitions are not universally valid because they fail when applied to a composite of different things. Another common definition—'unity is the absence of multiplicity, multiplicity is the aggregate of units'—is then evaluated. This definition is based on the epistemological priority: since unity is a principle (mabda') and reason knows the principle before its derivative, unity is better known to reason; since multiplicity is perceived by imagination which forms images, it is better known to imagination. Thus, interpreting unity via multiplicity in imagination and multiplicity via unity in reason is merely conventional (bi al-a'rāf), not a true explanation that imparts knowledge or removes ignorance.

الكثرة (al-kathra)
الانقسام (al-inqisām)
العقل (al-'aql)
الخيال (al-khayāl)
المبدأ (al-mabda')
التفسير بالعرف (al-tafsīr bi al-'urf)

B) Because they fail when applied to a composite of different things.
C) Because they are too complex.
D) Because they contradict reason.

Key Takeaway
Verbal definitions of unity and multiplicity are conventional, not true explanations, due to the different roles of reason and imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's oneness (tawḥīd), do you rely more on rational definitions or on imaginative images? After reading this passage, how might your approach be limited? Will you seek a deeper, less conventional understanding?
?
This lesson shows that definitions of unity and multiplicity are conventional, not absolute. In your own life, what cherished definitions (of success, happiness, or identity) might be merely conventional? Are you willing to question them?
?
If you often use terms like 'one' or 'many' without thinking, how does the critique of their definitions challenge your certainty? Will you now speak with more humility about concepts you thought you understood?

Philosophers' Classification of Pairs: Likes, Differents, Opposites
Philosophers' classification of pairs into likes, differents, and opposites with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers classify pairs of things into likes, differents, and opposites, and the conditions for each category.

Core Idea
Distinction is universal; likes share essence, differents do not, opposites cannot coexist.

This passage presents the philosophers' systematic classification of multiplicity, which is foundational for understanding how they analyze identity and difference. Before this, the theologians' view was discussed; now the commentator explains the philosophers' distinct approach. You will see how every pair is first classified as distinct, then further divided based on essence and coexistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the philosophers' classification.
Notice the categories: likes, differents, and opposites.
2
Identify the three categories
Find where the passage defines 'likes', 'differents', and 'opposites'. Note the specific conditions for each.
3
Understand the types of distinction
The passage also mentions real, accidental, and mental distinction. Relate these to the broader classification.
This expands the initial premise.
4
Apply the concept with examples
Think of examples: Zayd and Amr are likes; black and white are opposites; black and sweetness are differents but not opposites.
Examples help cement the distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وعند الفلاسفة كل اثنين فهما غيران، فإن اشتركا في تمام الماهية فمثلان وإلا فمتخالفان وهما متقابلان إن امتنع اجتماعهما في محل واحد من جهة واحدة. ما سبق من أقسام الكثرة وأحكامها على رأي المتكلمين، وأما على رأي الفلاسفة، فالكثرة تستلزم التغاير بمعنى أن كل اثنين فهما غيران، فإن كانت الأثنينية بالحقيقة، فبالحقيقة أو بالعارض فبالعارض، أو بالاعتبار فبالاعتبار، ثم الغيران إما أن يشتركا في تمام الماهية كزيد وعمرو في الإنسانية أو لا . فالأول المثلان، والثاني المتخالفان سواء اشتركا في ذاتي أو عرضي أو لم يشتركا أصلا، ثم المتخالفان قد يكونان متقابلين كالسواد والبياض، وقد لا يتقابلان كالسواد والحلاوة والمتقابلان هما المتخالفان اللذان يمتنع اجتماعهما في محل واحد في زمان واحد من جهة واحدة.
He said: According to the philosophers, every two things are distinct. If they share in the complete essence, they are likes; otherwise they are differents, and they are opposites if their coexistence in one subject from one aspect is impossible. What preceded are the divisions of multiplicity and their rulings according to the theologians. As for according to the philosophers, multiplicity entails distinction, meaning that every two things are distinct. Then if the duality is real, then real; or accidental, then accidental; or mental, then mental. Then the two distinct things either share in the complete essence, like Zayd and Amr in humanity, or not. The first are the likes, and the second are the differents, whether they share in an essential or accidental property or do not share at all. Then the differents may be opposites, like black and white, and may not be opposites, like black and sweetness. The opposites are the differents whose coexistence in one subject in one time and from one aspect is impossible.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' classification of multiplicity and distinction. It begins by quoting the original text that according to the philosophers, any two things are distinct (ghayran). They then classify these pairs: if they share the complete essence (e.g., both are human), they are likes (mithlayn); otherwise they are differents (mukhalifan). Among differents, some are opposites (mutaqabilan) if they cannot coexist in the same subject from the same aspect. The commentator then elaborates: The earlier discussion was on the theologians' view. For the philosophers, multiplicity entails distinction, and the distinction is either real, accidental, or mental. Then the two distinct things either share in the complete essence (likes) or not (differents), regardless of whether they share an essential or accidental property. Among differents, some are opposites (e.g., black and white) and some are not (e.g., black and sweetness). The definition of opposites is then given: two differents that cannot coexist in one subject, in one time, from one aspect.

Key Terms
غيران
مثلان
متخالفان
متقابلان
الكثرة
التغاير
تمام الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All pairs of different things (differents) are opposites.
Correction: Opposites are only a subset of differents; they require the additional condition of impossible coexistence in one subject at one time from one aspect.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is the condition for two distinct things (ghayran) to be considered opposites (mutaqabilan)?
A) They share the same complete essence.
B) They cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time in the same respect.
C) One must be existent and the other non-existent.
D) They must be like black and white.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers classify pairs into likes (share essence), differents (do not), and opposites (differents that cannot coexist in one subject).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a relationship where you have classified someone as fundamentally 'different' from you. Based on this lesson, are you focusing on accidental differences or on the complete essence (humanity) you share? How might remembering that you both share the same essence change how you treat that person? Commit to one concrete action today that reflects this truth.
?
Consider a belief or value you hold as absolutely 'opposite' to another viewpoint. Does this lesson's condition—'impossible to coexist in one subject from one aspect'—apply? Could it be that the two are not truly opposites but differ in aspect? Are you willing to honestly re-examine your assumption? What would it cost you to do so?

Definitions of Contrariety and Privation-Possession
Al-Taftazani distinguishes between logical and philosophical definitions of contrariety and privation-possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of contrariety and privation-possession used by philosophers, including the simple logical definitions and the more restricted philosophical definitions.

Core Idea
Philosophers added a restriction to logical definitions to make them more precise.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents the definitions of contrariety and privation-possession as they appear in logic and then as they are refined in philosophical discussions. The key is understanding the additional condition for each: for contraries, extreme difference; for privation, the subject's capability at the time.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of contrariety
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the definition of contrariety (tadād) in both its logical and philosophical versions. Note the condition of extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf).
The example of black and white illustrates the restricted definition.
2
Read the definitions of privation-possession
Now read the definition of privation-possession (malaka wa 'adam). Note the condition that privation must be the absence of a quality from a subject capable of it at that time.
The example of the beardless youth (kūsaj) vs the young boy (amrad) clarifies the condition.
3
Compare logical and philosophical versions
Compare the earlier (logical) definitions with the restricted (philosophical) ones. Notice how the philosophical versions add a condition, making them more specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما مر من تفسير التضاد وتفسير الملكة والعدم هو الذي أورده قدماء الفلاسفة في أوائل المنطق، وأما في مباحث الفلسفة فقد اعتبروا في كل منهما قيداً آخر وهو في المتضادين، أن يكون بينهما غاية الخلاف كالسواد والبياض، بخلاف البياض والصفرة وفي الملكة والعدم، أن يكون العدم سلب الوجود عما هو من شأنه في ذلك الوقت كعدم اللحية عن الكوسج بخلافه عن الأمرد، وكل من التضاد والملكة والعدم، بالمعنى الأول أعم منه بالمعنى الثاني ضرورة أن المطلق أعم من المقيد إلا أن المطلق من التضاد يسمى بالمشهوري لكونه المشهور فيما بين عوام الفلاسفة والمقيد بالحقيقي لكونه المعتبر في علومهم الحقيقية، والملكة والعدم بالعكس حيث يسمون المطلق بالحقيقي، والمقيد بالمشهوري، ولما كان تقابل مثل البياض مع الصفرة والسواد مع الحمرة ونحو ذلك مما ليس بينهما غاية الخلاف، وكذا تقابل البصر وعدمه عن العقرب أو الشجر قادحاً في حصر التقابل في الأقسام الأربعة لكونه خارجاً عن التضاد وعن الملكة والعدم بالمعنى الأخص . أجاب المتأخرون بأن الحصر إنما هو باعتبار المعنى الأهم أعني المشهوري التضاد، والحقيقي من الملكة والعدم ليدخل أمثال ذلك . وفيه نظر، أما أولاً فلأن الضدين في التضاد المشهوري لا يلزم أن يكونا وجوديين، بل قد يكون أحدهما عدماً للآخر كالسكون للحركة والظلمة للنور والمرض للصحة والعجمة للنطق، والأنوثة للذكورة، والفردية للزوجية . صرح بذلك ابن سينا وغيره فهو لا يكون قسيماً لتقابل الملكة والعدم، وتقابل الإيجاب والسلب، بل وفي كلامهم، أنه اسم يقع على التضاد الحقيقي، وعلى بعض أقسام الملكة والعدم، أعني ما يمكن فيه انتقال الموضوع من العدم إلى الملكة كالسكون والحركة بخلاف العمى والبصر، والحق أنه أعم من ذلك إذ لا يمكن الانتقال في النطق والعجمة، وفي الذكورة والأنوثة، وفي الزوجية والفردية، على أن تقابل الزوجية والفردية عند التحقيق راجع إلى الإيجاب والسلب، فإن الزوج عدد ينقسم بمتساويين، والفرد عدد لا ينقسم بمتساويين، فالأول اسم للموضوع، أعني العدد مع الإيجاب، والثاني اسم له مع السلب، كذا ذكره ابن سينا . وأما ثانياً: فلأنه صرح ابن سينا وغيره بأن غاية الخلاف شرط في التضاد المشهورى أيضاً، وحينئذ يكون تقابل مثل البياض والحمرة خارجاً عن الأقسام.
The earlier interpretation of contrariety and interpretation of privation and possession is what the ancient philosophers presented in the beginnings of logic. As for the discussions of philosophy, they considered in each of them another restriction: in the two contraries, that between them there be extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf), like black and white, unlike white and yellowish; and in privation and possession, that the privation be the negation of existence from that which is capable of it at that time, like the lack of a beard on a beardless youth, unlike on a young boy. And each of contrariety and privation-possession, in the first sense, is more general than it is in the second sense, necessarily because the absolute is more general than the restricted. However, the absolute of contrariety is called 'the popular' (al-mashhūrī) because it is well-known among the common philosophers, and the restricted is called 'the true' (al-ḥaqīqī) because it is what is considered in their true sciences. And privation and possession are the opposite, since they call the absolute 'the true' and the restricted 'the popular'. And since the opposition of something like white with yellowish and black with reddish and the like, which do not have between them extreme difference, and similarly the opposition of sight and its absence from a scorpion or a tree, would be a defect in the enumeration of opposition into the four divisions because it falls outside contrariety and outside privation and possession in the more specific sense. The later scholars answered that the enumeration is only with respect to the more important sense, namely the popular of contrariety and the true of privation and possession, so that such examples enter. But there is a problem. As for the first: because the two opposites in popular contrariety do not necessarily have to be existent, rather one of them may be the non-existence of the other, like rest for motion, darkness for light, illness for health, speech impediment for speech, femininity for masculinity, and oddness for evenness. Ibn Sina and others explicitly stated that. Thus it is not a correlative of the opposition of privation and possession and the opposition of affirmation and negation. Rather, in their statements, it is a name that applies to true contrariety and to some divisions of privation and possession, namely that in which the subject can transition from privation to possession, like rest and motion, unlike blindness and sight. The truth is that it is more general than that, since transition is not possible in speech impediment and speech, in masculinity and femininity, and in oddness and evenness. Moreover, the opposition of oddness and evenness, upon investigation, reduces to affirmation and negation: for an even number is a number that can be divided into two equals, and an odd number is a number that cannot be divided into two equals. So the first is a name for the subject, i.e., number with affirmation, and the second is a name for it with negation. Thus Ibn Sina mentioned it. As for the second problem: because Ibn Sina and others explicitly stated that extreme difference is also a condition in popular contrariety. Then the opposition of white and red falls outside the divisions.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the definitions of contrariety (taḍād) and privation-possession (malaka wa 'adam) as used by philosophers, and an examination of an objection regarding the completeness of the fourfold classification of opposition (taqābul). The author begins by recalling the definitions from the beginnings of logic: the earlier simple definitions (contrariety as maximal difference, privation as absence of what is naturally expected). In philosophy, a restriction is added: for contraries, there must be extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) like black and white, not white and yellowish; for privation-possession, the privation must be of a quality that the subject is suited to have at that time (e.g., a lack of beard on a beardless youth, not on a young boy). The absolute (unrestricted) sense of each is more general than the restricted sense. However, they name the absolute of contrariety 'popular' (mashhūrī) because common philosophers use it, and the restricted 'true' (ḥaqīqī) because it is used in their true sciences. For privation-possession, it is the opposite: they call the absolute 'true' and the restricted 'popular'. The objection is that examples like white vs. yellowish, black vs. reddish, and the presence vs. absence of sight in a scorpion or tree do not have extreme difference and are not privation-possession in the specific sense (since the subject is not suited for sight at that time). These would fall outside the fourfold division (contrariety, privation-possession, affirmation-negation, and correlation). Later scholars answered that the enumeration only considers the 'important' sense: the popular of contrariety and the true of privation-possession, so such examples are included. The author finds this problematic for two reasons. First reason: Ibn Sina and others stated that in popular contrariety, the two opposites need not both be existent; one can be the absence of the other (e.g., rest vs. motion, darkness vs. light). Thus popular contrariety overlaps with privation-possession and cannot be a mutually exclusive division. Also, Ibn Sina says that the term 'contrariety' sometimes applies to true contrariety and to some types of privation-possession where the subject can transition (like rest and motion, but not blindness and sight). In reality, the term is even more general, because transition is not possible in cases like speech impediment vs. speech, masculinity vs. femininity, oddness vs. evenness. Moreover, oddness vs. evenness reduces to affirmation and negation (since 'even' means divisible into equals, 'odd' means not divisible). So those examples are not outside the four divisions but belong elsewhere. Second reason: Ibn Sina and others also stated that extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) is a condition even in popular contrariety. If so, then white vs. red (which lack extreme difference) would still fall outside the divisions, and the later scholars' response fails. Thus the author critiques the later scholars' attempt to save the fourfold classification.

Key Terms
تضاد
ملكة وعدم
غاية الخلاف
سلب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Privation is simply any absence; there is no condition of capability.
Correction: In the philosophical sense, privation requires that the subject be capable of the quality at that time.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for privation-possession in the specific (philosophical) sense?
A) The privation must be the negation of existence from what is capable of it at that time.
B) The privation must be of an accidental property.
C) The subject must have never possessed the quality.
D) The privation must be permanent.

Key Takeaway
The philosophical definitions of contrariety and privation-possession add conditions of extreme difference and capacity, making them more specific than the logical definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel an absence in your life—whether of patience, knowledge, or virtue—do you examine whether you are truly capable of that quality at this time? How might that realistic self-assessment change your response to your own shortcomings?
?
Think of a belief you hold as an 'opposite' to another. Have you considered how much difference truly separates them? Are you willing to refine that belief by looking for extreme difference, or do you settle for vague opposition? What would that cost you—or free you from?

The Popular and True Senses
Al-Taftazani explains the naming conventions for two senses of contrariety and privation-possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how philosophers distinguish between the absolute (general) and restricted (specific) senses of contrariety and privation-possession, and the naming conventions.

Core Idea
The naming of senses (popular vs true) is reversed for contrariety and privation-possession.

This passage explains that the logical (absolute) sense of contrariety and privation-possession is more general than the philosophical (restricted) sense. Interestingly, philosophers refer to the absolute sense of contrariety as 'popular' and the restricted as 'true', while for privation-possession they reverse the labels. Understanding this terminology is crucial for following the later discussion.

Guided Path
1
Understand absolute vs restricted
The first sense (logical) is absolute and more general; the second sense (philosophical) is restricted and less general. This is a necessary relationship.
Think of 'animal' (more general) vs 'human' (more restricted).
2
Note the naming for contrariety
For contrariety, the absolute sense is called 'popular' (mashhūrī) because it is commonly used; the restricted is called 'true' (ḥaqīqī) because it is used in the true sciences.
This is stated explicitly: 'al-muṭlaq min al-taḍād yusammā bi-l-mashhūrī wa-l-muqayyad bi-l-ḥaqīqī'.
3
Note the naming for privation-possession
For privation-possession, the naming is reversed: the absolute is called 'true' and the restricted is called 'popular'.
The text says: 'wa-l-malaka wa-l-ʿadam bil-ʿaks'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما مر من تفسير التضاد وتفسير الملكة والعدم هو الذي أورده قدماء الفلاسفة في أوائل المنطق، وأما في مباحث الفلسفة فقد اعتبروا في كل منهما قيداً آخر وهو في المتضادين، أن يكون بينهما غاية الخلاف كالسواد والبياض، بخلاف البياض والصفرة وفي الملكة والعدم، أن يكون العدم سلب الوجود عما هو من شأنه في ذلك الوقت كعدم اللحية عن الكوسج بخلافه عن الأمرد، وكل من التضاد والملكة والعدم، بالمعنى الأول أعم منه بالمعنى الثاني ضرورة أن المطلق أعم من المقيد إلا أن المطلق من التضاد يسمى بالمشهوري لكونه المشهور فيما بين عوام الفلاسفة والمقيد بالحقيقي لكونه المعتبر في علومهم الحقيقية، والملكة والعدم بالعكس حيث يسمون المطلق بالحقيقي، والمقيد بالمشهوري، ولما كان تقابل مثل البياض مع الصفرة والسواد مع الحمرة ونحو ذلك مما ليس بينهما غاية الخلاف، وكذا تقابل البصر وعدمه عن العقرب أو الشجر قادحاً في حصر التقابل في الأقسام الأربعة لكونه خارجاً عن التضاد وعن الملكة والعدم بالمعنى الأخص . أجاب المتأخرون بأن الحصر إنما هو باعتبار المعنى الأهم أعني المشهوري التضاد، والحقيقي من الملكة والعدم ليدخل أمثال ذلك . وفيه نظر، أما أولاً فلأن الضدين في التضاد المشهوري لا يلزم أن يكونا وجوديين، بل قد يكون أحدهما عدماً للآخر كالسكون للحركة والظلمة للنور والمرض للصحة والعجمة للنطق، والأنوثة للذكورة، والفردية للزوجية . صرح بذلك ابن سينا وغيره فهو لا يكون قسيماً لتقابل الملكة والعدم، وتقابل الإيجاب والسلب، بل وفي كلامهم، أنه اسم يقع على التضاد الحقيقي، وعلى بعض أقسام الملكة والعدم، أعني ما يمكن فيه انتقال الموضوع من العدم إلى الملكة كالسكون والحركة بخلاف العمى والبصر، والحق أنه أعم من ذلك إذ لا يمكن الانتقال في النطق والعجمة، وفي الذكورة والأنوثة، وفي الزوجية والفردية، على أن تقابل الزوجية والفردية عند التحقيق راجع إلى الإيجاب والسلب، فإن الزوج عدد ينقسم بمتساويين، والفرد عدد لا ينقسم بمتساويين، فالأول اسم للموضوع، أعني العدد مع الإيجاب، والثاني اسم له مع السلب، كذا ذكره ابن سينا . وأما ثانياً: فلأنه صرح ابن سينا وغيره بأن غاية الخلاف شرط في التضاد المشهورى أيضاً، وحينئذ يكون تقابل مثل البياض والحمرة خارجاً عن الأقسام.
The earlier interpretation of contrariety and interpretation of privation and possession is what the ancient philosophers presented in the beginnings of logic. As for the discussions of philosophy, they considered in each of them another restriction: in the two contraries, that between them there be extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf), like black and white, unlike white and yellowish; and in privation and possession, that the privation be the negation of existence from that which is capable of it at that time, like the lack of a beard on a beardless youth, unlike on a young boy. And each of contrariety and privation-possession, in the first sense, is more general than it is in the second sense, necessarily because the absolute is more general than the restricted. However, the absolute of contrariety is called 'the popular' (al-mashhūrī) because it is well-known among the common philosophers, and the restricted is called 'the true' (al-ḥaqīqī) because it is what is considered in their true sciences. And privation and possession are the opposite, since they call the absolute 'the true' and the restricted 'the popular'. And since the opposition of something like white with yellowish and black with reddish and the like, which do not have between them extreme difference, and similarly the opposition of sight and its absence from a scorpion or a tree, would be a defect in the enumeration of opposition into the four divisions because it falls outside contrariety and outside privation and possession in the more specific sense. The later scholars answered that the enumeration is only with respect to the more important sense, namely the popular of contrariety and the true of privation and possession, so that such examples enter. But there is a problem. As for the first: because the two opposites in popular contrariety do not necessarily have to be existent, rather one of them may be the non-existence of the other, like rest for motion, darkness for light, illness for health, speech impediment for speech, femininity for masculinity, and oddness for evenness. Ibn Sina and others explicitly stated that. Thus it is not a correlative of the opposition of privation and possession and the opposition of affirmation and negation. Rather, in their statements, it is a name that applies to true contrariety and to some divisions of privation and possession, namely that in which the subject can transition from privation to possession, like rest and motion, unlike blindness and sight. The truth is that it is more general than that, since transition is not possible in speech impediment and speech, in masculinity and femininity, and in oddness and evenness. Moreover, the opposition of oddness and evenness, upon investigation, reduces to affirmation and negation: for an even number is a number that can be divided into two equals, and an odd number is a number that cannot be divided into two equals. So the first is a name for the subject, i.e., number with affirmation, and the second is a name for it with negation. Thus Ibn Sina mentioned it. As for the second problem: because Ibn Sina and others explicitly stated that extreme difference is also a condition in popular contrariety. Then the opposition of white and red falls outside the divisions.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the definitions of contrariety (taḍād) and privation-possession (malaka wa 'adam) as used by philosophers, and an examination of an objection regarding the completeness of the fourfold classification of opposition (taqābul). The author begins by recalling the definitions from the beginnings of logic: the earlier simple definitions (contrariety as maximal difference, privation as absence of what is naturally expected). In philosophy, a restriction is added: for contraries, there must be extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) like black and white, not white and yellowish; for privation-possession, the privation must be of a quality that the subject is suited to have at that time (e.g., a lack of beard on a beardless youth, not on a young boy). The absolute (unrestricted) sense of each is more general than the restricted sense. However, they name the absolute of contrariety 'popular' (mashhūrī) because common philosophers use it, and the restricted 'true' (ḥaqīqī) because it is used in their true sciences. For privation-possession, it is the opposite: they call the absolute 'true' and the restricted 'popular'. The objection is that examples like white vs. yellowish, black vs. reddish, and the presence vs. absence of sight in a scorpion or tree do not have extreme difference and are not privation-possession in the specific sense (since the subject is not suited for sight at that time). These would fall outside the fourfold division (contrariety, privation-possession, affirmation-negation, and correlation). Later scholars answered that the enumeration only considers the 'important' sense: the popular of contrariety and the true of privation-possession, so such examples are included. The author finds this problematic for two reasons. First reason: Ibn Sina and others stated that in popular contrariety, the two opposites need not both be existent; one can be the absence of the other (e.g., rest vs. motion, darkness vs. light). Thus popular contrariety overlaps with privation-possession and cannot be a mutually exclusive division. Also, Ibn Sina says that the term 'contrariety' sometimes applies to true contrariety and to some types of privation-possession where the subject can transition (like rest and motion, but not blindness and sight). In reality, the term is even more general, because transition is not possible in cases like speech impediment vs. speech, masculinity vs. femininity, oddness vs. evenness. Moreover, oddness vs. evenness reduces to affirmation and negation (since 'even' means divisible into equals, 'odd' means not divisible). So those examples are not outside the four divisions but belong elsewhere. Second reason: Ibn Sina and others also stated that extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) is a condition even in popular contrariety. If so, then white vs. red (which lack extreme difference) would still fall outside the divisions, and the later scholars' response fails. Thus the author critiques the later scholars' attempt to save the fourfold classification.

Key Terms
مطلق
مقيد
مشهوري
حقيقي
أعم

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the name given to the restricted sense of privation-possession?
A) True (ḥaqīqī)
B) Popular (mashhūrī)
C) Absolute (muṭlaq)
D) General (ʿāmm)

Key Takeaway
The naming of senses (popular vs true) is reversed for contrariety and privation-possession: absolute contrariety is popular, but absolute privation-possession is true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed a term meant one thing based on common usage, only to discover its technical meaning is the opposite? How does this passage encourage you to verify definitions before relying on them?
?
The philosophers revised their naming to reflect what was 'true' in their sciences. In your own study of Islam, are you content with popular understandings, or do you seek the precise meanings given by scholars? What term would you commit to investigating further this week?
?
This passage shows that even experts can use terms in reversed ways. When you encounter a difficult concept in aqeedah, what steps will you take to ensure you understand it as intended by the author, not as you assume?
