Objection to the Fourfold Classification and Critique
Al-Taftazani presents an objection to the fourfold classification and critiques the later scholars' answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that some cases of opposition fall outside the fourfold classification, the later scholars' response, and al-Taftazani's critique of that response.

Core Idea
The later scholars' defense of the fourfold classification fails due to overlapping definitions and conditions.

This passage presents an objection to the completeness of the fourfold classification of opposition (contrariety, privation-possession, affirmation-negation, and correlation). Examples like white vs. yellowish or sight vs. its absence in a scorpion seem to be outside the classification. The later scholars attempted to save the classification by appealing to the 'important' senses, but al-Taftazani critiques their response using arguments from Ibn Sina.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: examples like white vs yellowish (no extreme difference) and sight vs its absence from a scorpion (not proper privation) seem outside the four divisions.
These examples challenge the claim that all oppositions are covered.
2
Read the later scholars' response
They responded that the enumeration only considers the 'important' senses: popular contrariety and true privation-possession, thus including those examples.
This response attempts to salvage the classification.
3
Examine the first problem
Al-Taftazani first argues that in popular contrariety, one opposite can be the non-existence of the other (e.g., rest vs motion), causing overlap with privation-possession and affirmation-negation.
This means popular contrariety is not a separate, distinct division.
4
Examine the second problem
Ibn Sina also held that extreme difference is a condition even in popular contrariety, so examples like white vs red still fall outside the divisions.
Thus the later scholars' response fails on its own terms.

Source Text

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourfold classification of opposition is exhaustive and contrariety always involves two existent qualities.
Correction: Popular contrariety may involve a non-existent as an opposite, and examples lacking extreme difference or proper privation challenge the classification.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what problem does al-Taftazani raise regarding popular contrariety?
A) It requires both opposites to be existent.
B) It may involve one opposite being the non-existence of the other, causing overlap.
C) It is the same as true contrariety.
D) It only applies to substances.

Key Takeaway
The later scholars' defense of the fourfold classification fails because popular contrariety can include privation, and extreme difference is required in popular contrariety as well.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a criticism of a widely accepted classification (like the fourfold opposition), do you tend to defend it without truly examining the evidence, as the later scholars did? In what area of your faith or practice might you be clinging to a defense that doesn't hold up under scrutiny?
?
Al-Taftazani shows that even respected scholars' answers can be flawed. Are you willing to admit when your own reasoning about Islamic matters is inadequate, even if it was taught by respected figures? How can you develop the humility to revisit your conclusions?
?
This lesson reveals that overlap and ambiguity are real challenges in logical systems. How often do you accept clear-cut categories in your understanding of religion (e.g., halal/haram, believer/unbeliever) without considering the gray areas? What is one 'gray' area you need to investigate more deeply rather than forcing it into a simple category?

Taqābul and Taḍāyuf: Generality and Specificity
Al-Taftazani explains the relationship between opposition and correlation from two perspectives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani resolves the apparent conflict between the generality of opposition and correlation by distinguishing between the subject (actual instances) and the accident (concept).

Core Idea
Opposition is broader in scope than correlation, but narrower in definition.

This passage from al-Taftazani tackles a subtle logical point: how can opposition be both more general and more specific than correlation? The answer lies in whether we look at the actual things that are opposed (subject) or the concept of opposition itself (accident). Al-Taftazani uses this distinction to clarify the relationship and defend an earlier classification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Lemma
Read the opening statement: 'Opposition is general in subject, specific in accident.' This sets up the two perspectives.
The lemma is the starting point for the whole discussion.
2
Identify the Objection
The objector argues that correlation is more general than opposition, so how can opposition be a division of it? Notice the reasoning.
The objection rests on confusing the two perspectives.
3
Follow the Answer's First Part
Al-Taftazani concedes that correlation is more general in concept (accident), but this does not negate opposition's generality in subject.
The key is to separate 'what the things are' from 'how we conceive them'.
4
Examine the Example
Black and white are contraries (taḍād) but not correlatives; they are opposed in subject but not in concept.
This illustrates that opposition's subject is broader.
5
Synthesize the Two Perspectives
Realize that the apparent contradiction dissolves when we consistently apply the subject/accident distinction.
This is a crucial methodological tool in logic and kalam.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقابل عام بالمعروض خاص بالعارض قال : ومن حكم التقابل أنه أعم من التضايف باعتبار المعروض وأخص باعتبار العارض وأن التضاد قسيم التضايف وقسم منه باعتبارين . جواب عن اعتراض تقريره أن التضايف أعم من أن يكون تقابلاً أو تماثلاً أو تضاداً أو غير ذلك مما يدخل تحت المضاف، فكيف يجعل قسماً من التقابل أخص منه مطلقاً وقسيماً للتضاد منافياً له، وتقرير الجواب أن التضايف أعم من مفهوم التقابل العارض لأقسامه، ومفهوم التضاد العارض يمثل السواد والبياض ضرورة أنه لا يعقل المقابل أو المضاد إلا بالقياس إلى مقابل أو مضاد آخر، وهذا لا ينافي كون معروض التقابل أعم منه، بمعنى أن ما يصدق عليه التقابل قد يكونان متضايفين، وقد لا يكونان ومعروض التضاد مبايناً له كالسواد والبياض، فإنه لا تضايف بينهما . الذي هو قسم منه كما اقتضاه التقسيم السابق . ولكن ذلك العموم إنما هو باعتبار المعروض. أي مفهومه العارض للمفردات لا باعتبار المعروض بمعنى أن مفهوم التضايف أهم من مفهوم التقابل ضرورة أنه لا يعقل مفهوم تقابل في شيء إلا بالإضافة إلى تقابل في مقابله فيصدق على ذلك المفهوم مفهوم التضايف لاشتماله على توقف العقل فيه على شيئين . المذكورين وهما اعتبار المصدوق والمفهوم.
Opposition is general in terms of subject and specific in terms of accident. He said: And among the judgments of opposition is that it is more general than correlation (taḍāyuf) with respect to the subject (ma'rūḍ) and more specific with respect to the accident ('āriḍ), and that contrariety (taḍād) is a co-division of correlation and a division of it from two perspectives. This is an answer to an objection whose formulation is that correlation is more general than being an opposition, or a similarity, or a contrariety, or other than that which falls under the relative (muḍāf). So how can it be made a division of opposition that is more specific than it absolutely, and a co-division of contrariety that is opposite to it? The formulation of the answer is that correlation is more general than the concept of opposition that is an accident of its divisions, and the concept of contrariety that is an accident, like black and white, necessarily because an opposite or a contrary is not understood except in relation to another opposite or contrary. This does not contradict the fact that the subject of opposition is more general than it, meaning that that to which opposition applies may be two correlatives, or may not be, and the subject of contrariety is separate from it, like black and white, for there is no correlation between them. That [correlation] is a division of it [opposition], as required by the previous division. But that generality is only with respect to the subject. i.e., its concept that is an accident for individuals, not with respect to the subject, meaning that the concept of correlation is more important than the concept of opposition, necessarily because the concept of opposition is not understood in anything except by addition to an opposition opposite to it, so the concept of correlation applies to that concept because it includes the mind's dependence on two things. The two mentioned, namely considering the subject-matter (maṣdūq) and the concept (mafhūm).
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and defense of the relationship between opposition (taqābul) and correlation (taḍāyuf). The author first states a lemma: 'Opposition is general in terms of subject (ma'rūḍ) and specific in terms of accident ('āriḍ)'. This is followed by the commentary. The lemma also says that contrariety (taḍād) is both a co-division of correlation (since correlation is a genus under which fall opposition, similarity, etc.) and a division of it from two perspectives (presumably from the perspective of concept and subject). Then a potential objection is raised: correlation is more general than being either opposition, similarity, contrariety, etc., so how can it be a specific division of opposition, making it more specific than correlation, and also be a co-division of contrariety which is opposite to it? The answer is that correlation is more general than the concept ('āriḍ mafhūm) of opposition that occurs to its divisions, and also more general than the concept of contrariety. For example, black and white are contraries only in relation to each other; they require two things to be understood, which is a feature of correlation. So the concept of correlation is necessarily involved. However, this does not negate that the subject (ma'rūḍ) of opposition—i.e., the actual things that are opposed—can be more general than the subject of correlation, because some opposing things (like black and white) are not correlatives (there is no logical relation of 'the correlative of' between them). In other words, in terms of the actual things (maṣdūq) that fall under opposition, they are more numerous than those that fall under correlation. But in terms of the concept (mafhūm), correlation is more general because the very concept of opposition implies a relation between two things, which is a kind of correlation. Thus the apparent contradiction is resolved: from the perspective of the subject (actual instances), opposition is more general; from the perspective of the concept, correlation is more general.

تضايف
تضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that opposition and correlation are simply general and specific in the same sense.
Correction: The passage shows they differ based on whether one considers the subject (actual instances) or the accident (concept).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in what respect is opposition (taqābul) more general than correlation (taḍāyuf)?
A) In terms of the concept (mafhūm)
B) In terms of the subject (ma'rūḍ) or actual instances
C) In terms of both
D) In terms of neither

Key Takeaway
Opposition is more general than correlation in terms of actual instances (subject), but correlation is more general than opposition in terms of concept (accident).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quranic verses about Allah's attributes, do you sometimes confuse the concept (mafhūm) of an attribute with its instances (maṣdūq) in creation? How might this lead to anthropomorphism (tashbīh)? What specific step will you take today to purify your understanding of a particular attribute?
?
This lesson shows that two seemingly contradictory truths can both be correct when viewed from different angles. Consider a situation in your life where you have struggled to accept Allah's decree because your perspective was limited. Can you identify one such event and commit to trusting Allah's wisdom even when your human perspective sees contradiction?
?
The distinction between subject and accident forces you to examine your assumptions. In your daily worship, do you focus more on the external actions (subject) or the inner intention (accident)? Which one do you neglect, and what one change will you make in your next prayer to correct this balance?

The Ten Categories and the Claim That Existent Is Not a Genus
Taftazani presents the ten categories and asserts existent is not a genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Peripatetic doctrine of ten highest genera and the claim that existent is not a genus for substance and accident.

Core Idea
Existent is not a genus; it is predicated equivocally across categories.

Taftazani opens this section by presenting the Peripatetic doctrine of ten categories. He states that philosophers claim there are ten highest genera for contingent beings, with substance as the tenth. They argue that each is a true genus for what falls under it, and that existent is not a genus for substance or accident. This lesson introduces the categories and sets up the key claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim about ten categories
Read the passage carefully to identify the ten categories: substance and nine accidents. Note the variant views on the number of accident genera.
The passage mentions that some consider motion as an additional accident genus.
2
Understand the non-generic status of existent
Focus on the statement that existent is not a genus for substance and accident. This is a key doctrine that will be defended later.
3
Recognize the distinction between genus and universal accident
The passage states that each of the ten is a genus, not a universal accident. Understand the difference: a genus is essential, a universal accident is accidental.
4
Note the definitional basis
The meaning of substance is essence and reality, while accident is what occurs to a subject. This distinction will be used in the arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعموا أنها أجناس عالية عاشرها الجوهر، ويبتني على أن كلاً منها جنس وما تحته أجناس، وليس الموجود جنساً للجوهر والعرض ولا العرض للأعراض، ولا النسبة للنسبيات. وقيل أجناس الأعراض ثلاثة: الكم والكيف والنسبة وزاد بعضهم الحركة. والجمهور على أن الأينية من الأين، وقيل من أن ينفعل كغير الأينية. قال: وزعموا ذهب الجمهور من الحكماء إلى أن الأجناس العالية للممكنات عشرة وهي الأعراض التسعة والجوهر، ويسمونها المقولات العشر ومبنى ذلك على أن كلاً منها جنس لما تحته لا عرض عام وما تحته من الأقسام الأولية أجناس لا أنواع، وليس الموجود جنساً للجوهر والعرض ولا العرض جنساً للأعراض التسعة ولا النسبة لأقسامها السبعة. وبينوا ما يحتاج إليه من ذلك إلى البيان، بأن المعنى من الجوهر ذات الشيء وحقيقته، فيكون ذاتياً بخلاف العرض فإن معناه ما يعرض للموضوع، وعروض الشيء للشيء إنما يكون بعد تحقق حقيقته، فلا يكون ذاتياً لما تحته من الأفراد وإن جاز أن يكون ذاتياً لما فيها من الحصص كالماشي لحصصه العارضة للحيوانات. وكذا النسبة للنسبيات السبع فإنهم لا يعنون بها ما يدخل النسبة في ذواتها سوى الإضافة، فإنها نسبة مكررة على ما سيأتي الكلام في النسبة. وبأن الموجود لو كان ذاتياً لهما لما كان مقولاً بالتشكيك ولما أمكن تعقل شيء من الجواهر والأعراض مع الشك في وجوده، ولما احتاج اتصافه بالوجود إلى سبب كحيوانية الإنسان ولونية السواد، وتعريفهما بالموجود في موضوع والموجود لا في موضوع رسم باللازم ذلك فليس اللازم هو الوجود حتى يكون كل جوهر مثلاً موجوداً البتة، لأن معناه أنه ماهية إذا وجدت لم يكن في موضوع، وهذا المعنى هو اللازم له. وهذا مع ما فيه من ضعف مقدمات إثبات جنسية الجوهر ونفي جنسية العرض لا يفيد تمام المطلوب لجواز أن يكون للكل أو للبعض منها ذاتي مشترك هو الجنس، ولا معول سوى الاستقراء.
And they claimed that they are the highest genera, the tenth of which is substance, and it is based on the fact that each of them is a genus and what is under it are genera, and that existent is not a genus for substance and accident, nor is accident a genus for accidents, nor is relation for relatives. And it was said: the genera of accidents are three: quantity, quality, and relation, and some added motion. And the majority hold that 'whereness' belongs to the category of 'where', and it was said it belongs to the category of 'being acted upon' like others of 'whereness'. He said: And they claimed that the majority of the philosophers went to the view that the highest genera for contingent beings are ten, namely the nine accidents and substance, and they call them the ten categories. The basis of that is that each of them is a genus for what is under it, not a universal accident, and what is under them of primary divisions are genera, not species. And existent is not a genus for substance and accident, nor is accident a genus for the nine accidents, nor is relation for its seven divisions. And they explained what is needed of that for clarification, that the meaning of substance is the essence of a thing and its reality, so it is essential, unlike accident, for its meaning is what occurs to a subject, and the occurrence of a thing to a thing is only after the realization of its reality, so it is not essential for the individuals under it, though it may be essential for the portions within them, like 'walking' for its portions that occur to animals. And similarly relation for the seven relatives, for they do not mean by it that relation enters into their essences except for addition, for it is a repeated relation, as will come in the discussion of relation. And [they argued] that if existent were essential for both of them, it would not be said by equivocation, and it would not be possible to conceive any of the substances and accidents while doubting its existence, and its being qualified by existence would not need a cause like the animality of man and the blackness of black. And their definition of them as 'existent in a subject' and 'existent not in a subject' is a definition by a concomitant. So the concomitant is not existence itself such that every substance, for example, is necessarily existent, because its meaning is a quiddity which, if it exists, is not in a subject, and this meaning is the concomitant. And this, together with the weakness of the premises for proving the genus-ness of substance and denying the genus-ness of accident, does not fully achieve the desired, because it is possible that for all or some of them there is a common essential that is the genus. And there is no reliance except on induction.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the Peripatetic doctrine of ten categories (substance and nine accidents). Taftazani begins by stating the claim that there are ten highest genera, and that existent (al-mawjūd) is not a genus for substance and accident, nor is accident a genus for the individual accidents, nor is relation for its subdivisions. He notes a variant view that accident genera are only three (quantity, quality, relation), with some adding motion. He then returns to the mainstream view, explaining that the ten categories are based on each being a genus for what falls under it, not a universal accident. The main argument against making existent a genus proceeds as follows: (1) If existent were essential to both substance and accident, it would be predicated univocally, but it is predicated by equivocation (tashkīk). (2) One could conceive substances or accidents while doubting their existence, which would be impossible if existence were part of their essence. (3) The qualification by existence would not need an external cause if existence were essential. (4) Defining substance as 'existent not in a subject' and accident as 'existent in a subject' is a definition by concomitant (lāzim), not by essence; the concomitant is not existence itself but the property of not being in a subject (for substance) or being in a subject (for accident). He then criticizes the strength of the arguments for the genus-ness of substance and against the genus-ness of accident, stating that they are not conclusive because it remains possible that all or some categories share a common essential genus, and the only method to verify is induction (istiqrā').

Key Terms
الجوهر
العرض
الموجود
الجنس
المقولات العشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'existent' is a genus that includes substance and accident as species.
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that existent is not a genus; it is predicated equivocally, not univocally.

Checkpoint
How many highest genera do the philosophers claim?
A) Nine
B) Ten
C) Twelve
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
The ten categories are the highest genera, and existent is not a genus for any of them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage teaches that 'existent' is not a genus for substance and accident. How does this expose a hidden assumption you have about the nature of existence? Identify one area in your life where you inadvertently treat Allah's existence as similar to your own, and repent from that today.
?
If the human mind can only grasp ten categories for all beings, does that not show the poverty of our reason when faced with the Divine? What specific step will you take this week to humble your intellect before the infinite reality of Allah?

Arguments Against Existent as a Genus and Their Critique
Taftazani presents and critiques arguments that existent is not a genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four arguments used to prove that existent is not a genus and Taftazani's critique of their conclusiveness.

Core Idea
If existent were a genus, it would undermine essential predication and causal necessity.

In this passage, Taftazani details the philosophers' arguments against making existent a genus. These arguments focus on equivocal predication, conceivability without existence, the need for a cause, and definitions by concomitants. He then offers a critique, questioning the strength of the premises and suggesting that only induction can settle the matter.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of substance and accident
The passage first distinguishes substance (essence) from accident (what occurs to a subject). This is foundational for the arguments.
2
Identify the four arguments
List the four arguments: (1) univocal predication, (2) conceivability without existence, (3) need for a cause, (4) definitions by concomitants.
3
Analyze each argument's logic
For each, ask: what premise does it rely on? How does it show that existent cannot be a genus?
4
Examine Taftazani's critique
He says the premises are weak and the arguments do not fully prove the claim. The only reliable method is induction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

Lexical and Technical Definitions of 'Arad (Accident)
Taftazani presents quotations from Ibn Sina, Ghazali, and Khwarizmi defining 'arad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical meaning of 'arad and its technical definitions according to Ibn Sina, al-Ghazali, and al-Khwarizmi.

Core Idea
The term 'arad shifts from a fleeting appearance to a logical universal.

In this passage, Taftazani compiles definitions of the term 'arad (accident) from three major scholars: Ibn Sina, al-Ghazali, and al-Khwarizmi. Each definition highlights a different aspect of the concept, from its linguistic root meaning 'appearing and not lasting' to its technical usage in logic and ontology. As you read, notice how the term expands from a simple description of phenomena to a precise logical category.

Guided Path
1
Understand the lexical meaning
Read the first sentence: 'Arad of a thing: it appeared, became manifest, and did not last.' This gives the linguistic foundation.
The root 'arada means 'to appear'.
2
Examine Ibn Sina's dichotomy
Ibn Sina defines accident as an existent in a subject, contrasting it with substance (not in a subject). Note his emphasis on essence and subject.
This is a foundational ontological distinction.
3
Analyze al-Ghazali's homonymy
Al-Ghazali says 'arad is a homonym with three senses: in a subject, in a condition, and as a universal predicated non-essentially. Compare these to Ibn Sina's definition.
Homonymy means the term has multiple related meanings.
4
Consider al-Khwarizmi's distinguishing quality
Al-Khwarizmi defines 'arad as that by which a thing is distinguished from another not in its essence, like colors and temperatures.
This highlights the accidental vs essential distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عرض الشيء ظهر وبدا ولم يدم. قال ابن سينا: يقال عرض لكل موجود في موضوع، وقال أيضاً: كل ذات لم يكن في موضوع فهو جوهر، وكل ذات قوامها في موضوع فهي عرض (النجاة ٣٢٥). وقال الغزالي: العرض اسم مشترك، فيقال عرض لكل موجود في موضوع ويقال عرض لكل موجود في حل، ويقال عرض للمعنى الكلي المفرد المحمول على كثيرين حملاً غير مقوم. راجع معيار العلم طبعة مصر ص ١٩٤. وقال الخوارزمي: العرض هو ما يتميز به الشيء عن الشيء لا في ذاته كالبياض والسواد والحرارة والبرودة وغير ذلك مفاتيح العلوم ص ٨٦.
'Arad of a thing: it appeared, became manifest, and did not last. Ibn Sina said: 'Arad is said of every existent that is in a subject, and he also said: every essence that is not in a subject is a substance, and every essence whose subsistence is in a subject is an accident (al-Najat 325). Al-Ghazali said: 'Arad is a homonym; it is said of every existent that is in a subject, and it is said of every existent that is in a condition (ḥall), and it is said of the universal simple meaning predicated of many non-essential predication. See Mi'yār al-'Ilm, Egypt edition, p. 194. Al-Khwarizmi said: 'Arad is that by which a thing is distinguished from another thing not in its essence, like whiteness, blackness, heat, cold, and other than that, Mafātīḥ al-'Ulūm, p. 86.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and technical note on the term 'arad (accident). It first gives the linguistic meaning: something that appears and does not last. Then it cites Ibn Sina's technical definition: an accident is an existent that inheres in a subject, whereas a substance is an essence not in a subject. Al-Ghazali notes that 'arad is a homonym (ishtirāk), with three senses: (1) any existent in a subject, (2) any existent in a condition (perhaps a misprint), and (3) a universal meaning predicated of many non-essentially. Al-Khwarizmi defines it as the distinguishing quality of a thing not in its essence, such as colors and temperatures. These definitions show the range of the term in logic and ontology.

Key Terms
عرض
جوهر
موضوع
محل
اسم مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'arad always means an ephemeral quality; in logic it also includes universal accidents like 'animal' said of many.
Correction: Al-Ghazali's third sense shows 'arad can be a universal meaning predicated non-essentially, which includes logical accidents beyond transient qualities.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, what distinguishes a substance from an accident?
A) A substance has no subject, while an accident does.
B) A substance is eternal, an accident is temporary.
C) A substance is spatial, an accident is not.
D) A substance causes itself, an accident is caused.

Key Takeaway
The term 'arad has both a lexical sense (appearing and fleeting) and multiple technical senses in logic and philosophy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often describe yourself by your roles and qualities. Which of those are truly essential to who you are, and which are mere accidents that appear and fade? How can you begin to see yourself as your substance (your soul) rather than your fleeting attributes?
?
If 'arad includes both physical qualities like whiteness and logical universals like 'animal', what does that reveal about the way you categorize things? Are you too quick to treat temporary traits as fixed essences in your judgments of others? What would it mean to judge your own actions as accidental rather than defining your core?

Linguistic Meaning of 'Arad and Its Persistence
Defines the linguistic sense of 'arad and critiques its persistence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic meaning of 'arad (accident) and why the idea of its persistence is problematic in kalam.

Core Idea
'Arad linguistically means temporary, not that which needs a substrate.

Before diving into the technical definition of 'arad in kalam, it is crucial to understand its linguistic roots. Sanusi clarifies that in Arabic, 'arad does not originally denote something that cannot subsist by itself; rather, it means something transient. He then critiques the notion that accidents can persist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on linguistic meaning
Notice how Sanusi distinguishes everyday usage from technical terminology.
2
Understand the two arguments against persistence
Identify the two possibilities for an accident's persistence and why both are weak.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this linguistic analysis supports the kalam definition of 'arad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال عرض لفلان أمر أي معنى لا قرار له وهذا أمر عارض، وهذه الحالة ليست بأصيلة، بل عارضة، ولهذا يسمى السحاب عارضاً، وليس اسماً لما لا يقوم بذاته، بل يفتقر إلى محل يقوم به، إذ ليس في معناه اللغوي ما ينبيء عن هذا المعنى . الثاني، أنه لو بقي فإما ببقاء محله فيلزم أن يدوم بدوامه لأن الدوام هو البقاء، وأن يتصف بسائر صفاته من التحيز والتقوم بالذات، وغير ذلك لكونها من توابع البقاء. وإما ببقاء آخر فيلزم أن يمكن فناء المحل ضرورة أنه لا تعلق لبقائه ببقائه، وكلا الوجهين في غاية الضعف، لأن العروض في اللغة إنما ينبيء عن عدم الدوام لا عن عدم البقاء في زمانين وأكثر، ولو سلم فلا يلزم في المعنى المصطلح عليه اعتبار هذا المعنى بالكلية، ولأن بقاءه ببقاء آخر لا يستلزم إمكان بقائه مع فناء المحل الجواز أن يكون بقاؤه مشروطاً ببقاء المحل كوجوده بوجوده.
It is said: an affair occurs to someone, meaning a meaning that has no stability. And this is a transient matter, and this state is not original but transient. For this reason, clouds are called 'arid (passing). And it is not a name for that which does not subsist by itself, but rather needs a substrate in which it subsists, since its linguistic meaning does not indicate this meaning. Second: if it remains, then either by the persistence of its substrate, then it would follow that it endures by its endurance because endurance is persistence, and that it is characterized by its other attributes of occupying space and subsisting by itself, etc., because they are among the consequences of persistence. Or by another persistence, then it would follow that the annihilation of the substrate is possible since its persistence has no connection to its persistence. And both aspects are extremely weak, because 'urud (accidentality) in language only indicates non-endurance, not non-persistence in two or more times. And even if conceded, it does not necessitate considering this meaning entirely in the terminological sense. And because its persistence by another persistence does not entail the possibility of its persistence with the annihilation of the substrate; it is possible that its persistence is conditional on the persistence of the substrate, just as its existence is conditional on its existence.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage begins by clarifying the linguistic meaning of 'arad (accident). The author explains that in language, 'arad means something that occurs temporarily, not something that has no stability. It is not originally a term for that which does not subsist by itself but needs a substrate; that is a later technical meaning. Then the author critiques the idea that accidents can persist. He presents two possible ways an accident could persist: either by the persistence of its substrate (meaning it lasts as long as the substrate lasts) or by a separate persistence. Both are shown to be weak. The linguistic meaning of 'urud indicates non-endurance, not non-persistence over multiple times. Even if we accept the possibility, it does not necessitate the technical meaning. Furthermore, persistence by another persistence does not imply the accident can remain when the substrate is annihilated; it may be that the accident's persistence is conditional on the substrate's persistence, just as its existence is conditional on the substrate's existence.

Key Terms
عرض
بقاء
عروض
دوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'arad in language means 'that which does not subsist by itself'.
Correction: The author shows it originally means 'temporary' or 'accidental', not the technical definition.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the linguistic meaning of 'arad (عرض)?
A) It means that which does not subsist by itself.
B) It means something that has stability.
C) It means something temporary or accidental.
D) It means a substance.

Key Takeaway
The linguistic meaning of 'arad emphasizes temporariness, not dependence on a substrate, and the idea of persistence is linguistically and philosophically problematic.

Defining Quantity: The Proper Special Property
Analysis of the correct property for defining quantity and refutation of alternatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the correct special property for defining quantity is not equality or acceptance of division, and the reasoning behind the Imam's (al-Razi's) analysis.

Core Idea
Defining quantity requires a non-circular property that applies to both continuous and discrete quantities.

This passage delves into the classical debate on how to properly define quantity (al-kamm). The author, following Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, examines two candidate properties: equality and acceptance of division, and finds both inadequate. He then clarifies the nature of 'acceptance' and refutes a misunderstanding about divisive division. As you read, pay attention to the logical rigor and the distinction between potential and actual division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow. Note the example of the cubit and fistfuls.
This example illustrates the hierarchical nature of measurement.
2
Identify the two rejected properties
The author rejects two special properties: equality (musāwāh) and acceptance of division (qabūl al-qisma). Understand why each is rejected.
Equality leads to circularity; acceptance of division fails for discrete quantity.
3
Analyze the nuance about acceptance
The author distinguishes between actual division (in discrete) and potential division (in continuous). Consider how broadening the meaning of 'acceptance' might save the definition, but then it becomes ambiguous.
This shows the importance of precise terminology.
4
Refute the misunderstanding about divisive division
The author corrects the notion that the Imam meant divisive division (infikākī), which is impossible for magnitude. Instead, magnitude only prepares matter for division.
Use the analogy of motion preparing a body for rest.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
The passage does not state the correct property explicitly, but it eliminates two candidates and clarifies key distinctions. Ask yourself: what property might be left?
The text implies a third property is the correct one, though not named here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك الذراع يعد بالقبضات والقبضة بالأصابع، والأصبع بالشعيرات، والشعيرة بالشعرات. وذكر الإمام أن هذه الخاصة هي التي تصلح لتعريف الكم بها لا الأولى، لأن المساواة لا تعرف إلا بالاتفاق في الكمية فيكون تعريف الكم بها دور، إلا أن يقال المساواة واللامساواة مما يدرك بالحس، لكن مع المحل لا مفرداً فإنه لا ينال إلا بالعقل فقصد تعريف ذلك المعقول بهذا المحسوس، ولا بالثانية لأن قبول القسمة من عوارض الكم المتصل لا المنفصل، فلا يشمله التعريف فلا ينعكس . وأرى أنه بنى ذلك على أن قبول الشيء عبارة عن إمكان حصوله من غير حصول بالفعل، ولا شك أن الانقسام في الكم المنفصل حاصل بالفعل، وأما إذا أريد بالقبول أعم من ذلك أعني إمكان فرض شيء غير شيء فلاخفاء في شموله المتصل والمنفصل ولذا قال الإمام إن قبول القسمة من عوارض المتصل دون المنفصل إلا إذا أخذ القبول باشتراك الاسم، وأما ما وقع في أنه كأنه أخذ القسمة الانفكاكية فسهو ظاهر، لأن الإمام قد صرح في هذا الموضع بأن القسمة الانفكاكية يستحيل عروضها للمقدار إذ عندها لا يبطل المقدار ويحدث مقداران آخران. نعم المقدار يهيء المادة لقبول الانقسام، لكن لا يلزم حصول ذلك في نفس المقدار، ولا بقاء المقدار عند حصول الانقسام، كالحركة تهيىء الجسم للسكون الطبيعي، ولا تبقى معه.
And likewise the cubit is counted by fistfuls, and the fistful by fingers, and the finger by hairs, and the hair by fine hairs. And the Imam mentioned that this specific property (al-khāṣṣa) is the one suitable to define al-kamm thereby, not the first, because equality is not known except by agreement in quantity; thus defining al-kamm by it would be circular, unless it is said that equality and inequality are perceived by sense, but together with the subject not separately, for it is not apprehended except by the intellect, so the intention was to define that intelligible by this sensible; nor by the second because acceptance of division (qabūl al-qisma) is from the accidents of continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttaṣil), not discrete, so the definition would not include it and would not be convertible. And I think he based that on the fact that accepting a thing is an expression of the possibility of its occurrence without actual occurrence, and there is no doubt that division in discrete quantity is actual; but if acceptance is intended to be more general than that, i.e., the possibility of assuming something other than something, then there is no concealment in its inclusion of both continuous and discrete. Therefore the Imam said that acceptance of division is an accident of continuous only, not discrete, unless acceptance is taken by shared name. And as for what occurred in that he apparently took the divisive division (al-qisma al-infikākiyya), it is a clear oversight, because the Imam declared in this place that the divisive division is impossible to occur to magnitude, because by it the magnitude would not cease and two other magnitudes would come into existence. Yes, magnitude prepares matter for acceptance of division, but its occurrence in the magnitude itself is not necessary, nor does the magnitude remain when division occurs, like motion prepares the body for natural rest, and does not remain with it.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the proper definition of quantity (al-kamm) and which special property (al-khāṣṣa) should be used to define it. The author begins by giving an example of hierarchical measurement (cubit, fistful, finger, etc.) to illustrate the practical notion of quantity. He then reports the Imam's (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) position that the correct property for definition is not the first one considered (probably equality/musāwāh) because defining quantity by equality would be circular—equality is known only by agreement in quantity. The author considers a possible objection that equality and inequality are perceptible by sense, but replies that they are perceived only with a subject, not in isolation, so the definition would still be circular. The second candidate property, acceptance of division (qabūl al-qisma), is also rejected because it is an accident only of continuous quantity, not discrete, so the definition would not cover discrete quantity and thus would not be convertible. The author then analyzes the basis of the Imam's reasoning, explaining that 'acceptance' ordinarily means possibility of actual division, but in discrete quantity division is actual, not potential. However, if acceptance is taken more broadly as the possibility of assuming something else, then it applies to both continuous and discrete, but then the Imam's statement that acceptance of division is specific to continuous depends on a shared name (i.e., ambiguity). The author then refutes a misunderstanding that the Imam meant divisive division (infikākī), which is impossible for magnitude. Finally, he clarifies that magnitude only prepares matter for division, but does not itself undergo division or remain after division, analogous to motion preparing a body for rest.

Key Terms
الكم
الخاصة
المساواة
قبول القسمة
الكم المتصل
الكم المنفصل
القسمة الانفكاكية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Imam's acceptance of division refers to divisive division (al-qisma al-infikākiyya) of magnitude.
Correction: The Imam did not mean divisive division, which is impossible for magnitude; he meant that magnitude prepares matter for division, not that it is itself divided.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the first special property (equality) for defining quantity?
A) Because equality is not a property of quantity.
B) Because defining quantity by equality would be circular, since equality is known by agreement in quantity.
C) Because equality only applies to discrete quantity.
D) Because equality is perceptible only by sense.

Key Takeaway
The proper special property for defining quantity must be non-circular and apply to both continuous and discrete quantities; equality and acceptance of division both fail.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a core belief in your understanding of Allah (e.g., His oneness, His attributes). Can you define it without using a term that already presupposes the concept? If you find yourself being circular, what does that reveal about the depth of your understanding? Commit to refining that definition this week.
?
The passage distinguishes between actual division and potential division. In your spiritual life, do you treat your relationship with Allah as something already fixed (actual) or as a potentiality that requires continuous growth? If it is potential, what concrete step will you take today to move from potential to actual obedience?

When you encounter a complex theological concept, do you tend to accept a vague or circular explanation out of comfort? Recall a specific instance. Classification of Quantity into Continuous and Discrete
Formal classification of quantity with definitions of time, line, surface, and solid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the formal classification of quantity into continuous and discrete, including the subcategories of time, line, surface, and body.

Core Idea
Quantity is either continuous or discrete; continuous splits into time and magnitude, and magnitude into line, surface, and body.

In this passage, the author presents a systematic classification of quantity (al-kamm), a key concept in Islamic philosophy and kalam. He distinguishes between continuous and discrete quantity, then further divides continuous into time and magnitude, and magnitude into line, surface, and teaching body. This classification is foundational for discussions of space, time, and number.

Guided Path
1
Understand the primary division
Read the passage and identify the two main categories: continuous and discrete. Note the defining criterion: having a common boundary or not.
The passage states that quantity either has a common boundary for its assumed parts or not.
2
Explore subdivisions of continuous
Continuous is divided into non-stationary (time) and stationary (magnitude). Magnitude is further divided into line, surface, and body based on the number of dimensions it accepts division.
Time is continuous but its parts do not coexist; magnitude's parts coexist.
3
Understand discrete quantity
Discrete quantity is identified as number alone, composed of units. Other things are only discretely in a derivative sense.
Number is the only genuine discrete quantity according to this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والمتصل بخلافه فإن كان غير قار فزمان وإلا فمقدار خط إن فقط قبل القسمة في جهة فقط وسطح أن قبلهما في جهيتن وجسم تعليمي إن قبلها في الجهات . قال والمتصل من أحكام الكم انقسامه إلى المتصل والمنفصل، ثم المتصل إلى أقسامه فالكم إما أن يكون لأجزائه المفروضه حد مشترك، أو لا. الثاني : المنفصل وهو العدد لا غير، لأن حقيقته ما يجتمع من الوحدات بالذات ولا معنى للعدد سوى ذلك، وغيره إنما يتصف بذلك لكونه معروضاً للعدد لكون أجزائه معروضاً للوحدة كالقول الذي يوهم أنه كم منفصل على ما يستحق في بحث الحروف والأول المتصل وهو إما أن يكون قار الذات أي مجتمع الأجزاء في الوجود، أو لا، الثاني الزمان والأول المقدار وهو إن فقط قبل القسمة في وجهة واحدة فقط فخط، وإن قبلها في جهتين فسطح، وإن قبلها في جهات فجسم تعليمي، فالخط امتداد واحد لا يحتمل إلا تجزئة في جهة والسطح امتداد يحتمل التجزئة في جهة وأمكن أن يعارضها تجزئة أخرى قائمة عليها حتى يمكن فيها فرض بعدين على قوائم، ولا يمكن غير ذلك، والجسم يحتمل التجزئة في ثلاث جهات، وحقيقته كمية ممتدة في الجهات المتناهية بالسطح الواحد المحيط ، أو بالسطوح لها باعتبار كل جهة
He said: And the continuous is opposite to it; if it is non-stationary then time, otherwise magnitude: a line if it accepts division in one dimension only, and a surface if it accepts division in two dimensions, and a teaching body if it accepts division in the dimensions. He said: And from the rulings of quantity is its division into continuous and discrete, then the continuous into its divisions. For quantity either has a common boundary for its assumed parts, or not. The second is discrete, and it is number only, because its reality is what is composed of units essentially, and there is no meaning for number other than that; other than it is described by that due to its being a substrate of number due to its parts being substrates of unity, like speech which suggests that it is discrete quantity as will be deserved in the discussion of letters. And the first is continuous, and it is either stationary in essence, i.e., with its parts co-existing in existence, or not; the second is time, and the first is magnitude. And it is: if it accepts division in one dimension only, then a line; if it accepts division in two dimensions, then a surface; if it accepts division in three dimensions, then a teaching body. So the line is a single extension that does not admit of division except in one dimension; the surface is an extension that admits of division in one dimension, and it is possible for another division to intersect it standing upon it, such that two dimensions can be assumed at right angles, and no other is possible; and the body admits of division in three dimensions, and its reality is a quantity extended in finite dimensions by the single encompassing surface, or by the surfaces according to each dimension.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a formal classification of quantity (al-kamm) into its main divisions. It begins with a quotation ("He said") stating that continuous (al-muttaṣil) is opposite to discrete (al-munfaṣil): if it is non-stationary (ghayr qārr), it is time (zamān); otherwise it is magnitude (miqdār), which includes line (khaṭṭ) if it accepts division in one dimension, surface (saṭḥ) if in two, and teaching body (jism ta‘līmī) if in three. Then a second quotation elaborates: the divisions of quantity are a ruling (aḥkām) of quantity itself. Quantity either has a common boundary (ḥadd mushtarak) for its assumed parts or not; the latter is discrete, which is only number (al-‘adad) whose essence consists of units; other things are only described as discrete because they are substrates of number. The former (continuous) is either stationary in essence (qārr al-dhāt), meaning its parts coexist, or not; the non-stationary is time, the stationary is magnitude. Magnitude then is subdivided: line (extension in one dimension), surface (extension in two dimensions, with possibility of perpendicular intersection), and body (extension in three dimensions). The passage concludes by describing the reality of a body as a quantity extended in finite dimensions bounded by surfaces.

Key Terms
المتصل
المنفصل
العدد
الزمان
المقدار
خط
سطح
جسم تعليمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Continuous quantity only applies to physical objects like bodies.
Correction: This passage clearly includes time as a continuous quantity, even though its parts do not coexist. Also, mathematical magnitudes (line, surface, body) are continuous even if not physical.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the definition of discrete quantity (al-munfaṣil)?
A) It is what has a common boundary for its parts.
B) It is number only, composed of units essentially.
C) It is a quantity extended in finite dimensions.
D) It is any quantity that accepts division.

Key Takeaway
Quantity is primarily divided into continuous (which includes time and magnitude) and discrete (which is number alone).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience time as fleeting and unfixed. This lesson defines time as non-stationary continuous quantity whose parts do not coexist. How does this definition change the way you perceive your own moments? Will you now see each moment as a discrete unit in a continuous flow that you cannot hold? What will you do today to treat each moment as a precious part of that flow that you must use wisely?
?
The lesson states number (discrete quantity) is composed of units. Your days are counted and your deeds accounted. Are you living each day as a distinct unit that will be added to the count of your life, or do you let them blur into a continuous indistinct stream? Which perspective makes you more accountable? Commit to treating today as a unit you will answer for.
?
Your physical body is defined by extension in three dimensions. But this lesson calls it a 'teaching body' — a model for understanding quantity, not a permanent reality. Does this detachment from the physical help you realize the temporary nature of your form? How does this shift your attachment to your body and your focus toward the eternal soul?

Definition of al-Miqdar and Its Two Senses
Defines magnitude (al-miqdar) and distinguishes pure quantity from quantity with relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two senses of al-miqdar (magnitude) as pure quantity vs. quantity with relation, and how length, width, and depth can be understood in both ways.

Core Idea
Length, width, and depth can be pure extensions or relational attributes depending on context.

In this passage, the author introduces a key term in kalam physics: al-miqdar (magnitude). He points out that terms like length, width, and depth can be used in two different ways. Understanding this distinction is crucial for later discussions on bodies, place, and space.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of 'قد يوجد مع إضافة' and 'كميات محضة'.
2
Identify the two senses of al-miqdar
The passage presents two ways to understand length, width, and depth. Find the phrases that distinguish pure quantities from quantities with addition.
The key phrase is 'قد يؤخذ يعني أنه قد يراد...'
3
Examine the examples for each sense
For the second sense (with addition), the author gives specific examples like length from head to feet or from center to circumference. Note how these are relational.
4
Understand the logical consequence
Because they are relational, these terms can be denied of some objects (e.g., this line is long, that is not). This would not be possible if they were pure quantities.
This connects to the idea that pure quantities are absolute, not relative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: المقدار قد يوجد مع إضافة ويسمى الطول والعرض والعمق. قال: والمقدار قد يؤخذ يعني أنه قد يراد بالطول والعرض والعمق نفس الامتدادات على ما مر فتكون كميات محضة، وقد يراد بالطول البعد المفروض أولاً أو أطول الامتدادات، أو البعد المأخوذ من رأس الإنسان إلى قدميه، أو الحيوان إلى ذنبه أو من مركز الكرة إلى محيطها، وبالعرض البعد المعروض ثانياً، أو أقصر البعدين أو البعد الآخذ من يمين الحيوان إلى شماله، وبالعمق البعد المفروض ثالثاً والمتحيز [؟] المعتبر من أعلى الشيء إلى أسفله أو فيما بين ظهر الحيوان وبطنه، وحينئذ لا تكون كميات محضة بل مأخوذة مع إضافات، ولهذا يصح سلبها عن الامتداد كما يقال هذا الخط طويل، وذاك ليس بطويل، وذلك السطح عريض، وذاك ليس بعريض.
He said: al-miqdar may exist with an addition and is called length, width, and depth. He said: al-miqdar may be taken, meaning that by length, width, and depth may be intended the extensions themselves as previously, so they are pure quantities. And may be intended by length: the first assumed dimension, or the longest of the extensions, or the dimension taken from a human's head to his feet, or from an animal to its tail, or from the center of a sphere to its circumference. And by width: the assumed second dimension, or the shorter of the two dimensions, or the dimension taken from the animal's right to its left. And by depth: the assumed third dimension, and the [؟] considered from the top of a thing to its bottom, or between the animal's back and belly. Then they are not pure quantities but taken with additions, and for this reason it is valid to negate them from extension, as it is said: this line is long, and that is not long; this surface is wide, and that is not wide.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage defines al-miqdar (magnitude) and clarifies its two senses. First, it can be taken as pure extension (al-imtidād) without any relation, in which case length, width, and depth are pure quantities. Second, it can be taken with an addition (iḍāfa), i.e., relative to a subject, such as the length from head to toe, the width from right to left, or the depth from top to bottom. In the second sense, terms like 'long' and 'wide' can be predicated of some things and denied of others, showing they are not pure quantities but relational attributes.

Key Terms
المقدار
الطول
العرض
العمق
كميات محضة
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Length, width, and depth are always absolute measures of extension.
Correction: In kalam, these terms can refer to either pure extension (absolute) or relational attributes (relative to a subject), as explained by Sanusi.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the difference between pure quantities and quantities taken with addition?
A) Pure quantities exist independently, while quantities with addition are imaginary.
B) Pure quantities are extensions themselves, while quantities with addition are relative to a subject.
C) Pure quantities are measurable, while quantities with addition are not.
D) Pure quantities are three-dimensional, while quantities with addition are only two-dimensional.

Key Takeaway
Al-miqdar can be understood as pure extension or as relational attributes, and this distinction is essential for applying terms like long and wide to objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe someone as 'short', are you making an absolute statement or a relational one? How does your awareness of this distinction change how you speak about others tomorrow?
?
Look at the room you are in. Measure its length in your mind. Is that length a pure number, or does it depend on how you define 'length'—from wall to wall, or from where you sit to the farthest corner? How does this lesson affect your trust in everyday measurements?
?
You habitually call things 'long', 'wide', or 'deep' without thinking. After reading this lesson, pick one object you use today and ask: is its magnitude a pure quantity or relational? What would it mean for your understanding of reality if all magnitudes were relational?

The Self-Evident Existence of Time
The author states time's existence is self-evident and that obscurity lies only in its reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that time's existence is self-evident and commonly acknowledged, and that the real dispute concerns its ultimate reality.

Core Idea
Time's existence is undeniable; only its true nature is debated.

The study of time often begins with a surprising claim: that everyone, from the scholar to the layperson, already accepts that time exists. The real puzzle is not whether time is real, but what it actually is. In this passage, the author sets the stage by affirming the self-evidence of time's existence and pointing toward the deeper inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening assertion
Read the first part of the passage carefully. Notice how the author states that the obscurity is only in the reality of time, not its existence. This is the key framing.
The word 'ضروري' (self-evident) is critical.
2
Identify the evidence for self-evidence
The author gives two pieces of evidence: common people acknowledge time's existence, and time is divided into years, months, days, and hours. Reflect on why these support self-evidence.
3
Connect to the broader discussion
Understand that this lesson clears the ground: the controversy that follows is about the 'reality' of time, not its existence. This is the author's methodological move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما الخفاء في حقيقته اختلاف المتكلمين والفلاسفة في حقيقة الزمان قال : فزعموا أن المقصود التنبيه وإلا فوجود امتداد يتصف بالمضي والاستقبال ضروري يعترف به العامة وتقسيمه إلى السنين والشهور والأيام والساعات، وإنما الخفاء في حقيقته، فزعم البعض أنه متجدد معلوم يقدر به متجدد موهوم وربما يتعاكس بحسب علم المخاطب كما يقال حين قعد عمرو في جواب متى قام زيد وبالعكس، ولا يخفي أن ليس في هذا إفادة تصور وذهب أرسطو وأتباعه إلى أنه مقدار حركة الفلك الأعظم لأنه لتفاوته كم ولا متناع تألفه من الآنات المتتالية لاستلزامه الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ متصل ولعدم استقراره مقدار لهيئة غير قارة وهي الحركة ولامتناع فنائه ضرورة أن بعدية العدم لا تكون إلا بالزمان مقدراً لحركة مستديرة إذا المستقيمة يجب انقطاعها لما سيأتي، ولتقدر جميع الحركات به مقدار لأسرعها الذي هو الحركة اليومية إذ الأكبر يقدر بالأصغر، والاكثر بالأقل كالفرسخ بالذراع والمائة بالعشرات دون العكس ورد ذلك بأنه مع الابتناء على الأصول الفاسدة، إنما يتم إن لو كان قبوله التفاوت لذاته
And the obscurity is only in its reality. The difference between the mutakallimun and philosophers concerning the reality of time. He said: They claimed that the purpose is a reminder; otherwise, the existence of an extension characterized by past and future is self-evident, acknowledged by the common people, and its division into years, months, days, and hours. And the obscurity is only in its reality. So some claimed that it is a known thing that is renewed by which an imaginary thing that is renewed is measured, and perhaps they are reversed according to the knowledge of the interlocutor, as it is said: 'When Amr sat down' in response to 'When did Zaid stand up?' and vice versa. And it is not hidden that this does not provide a conception. And Aristotle and his followers held that it is the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere, because it varies in quantity, and because its composition from consecutive instants is impossible due to entailing the indivisible part of a continuum, and because it is unstable as a measure for a non-fixed form, which is motion, and because its annihilation is impossible, given that posteriority to non-existence is only by time, measuring a circular motion, since rectilinear motion must terminate as will come. And all motions are measured by it as a measure for the fastest, which is the daily motion, for the larger is measured by the smaller and the more by the less, like the farsakh by the cubit and the hundred by the tens, not the opposite. And this was refuted by saying that, while based on false principles, it would be valid only if its susceptibility to variation were essential.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage first asserts that time's existence is self-evident (daruri), acknowledged by all, and the only obscurity is in its reality (haqiqa). It then presents two opinions on that reality. The first opinion (attributed to some philosophers) says time is a known thing that is renewed (mutajaddid ma'lum) by which an imaginary renewed thing (mutajaddid mawhum) is measured, and the two may be reversed depending on the interlocutor's knowledge—e.g., answering 'when did Zaid stand?' with 'when Amr sat' or vice versa. This does not provide a true conception. The second opinion (Aristotle and his followers) states time is the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere, with several arguments: it varies in quantity, cannot consist of consecutive instants (which would entail atoms), is unstable as a measure for a non-fixed form (motion), cannot annihilate because posteriority to non-existence requires time, and all motions are measured by the fastest motion (daily motion), like measuring larger by smaller. The passage ends with a brief refutation: this view is based on false principles, and would only hold if time's susceptibility to variation were essential (which is not proven).

Key Terms
امتداد
ضروري
تقسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the dispute is about whether time exists at all.
Correction: The passage clarifies that time's existence is self-evident and agreed upon; the obscurity is only about its reality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of time's existence?
A) It is known only through philosophical proof
B) It is self-evident and acknowledged by the common people
C) It is a matter of disagreement among theologians
D) It is purely imaginary

Key Takeaway
Time's existence is self-evident and universally accepted; the real intellectual inquiry concerns its reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience time every moment—its passing, its divisions. But have you ever stopped to ask what time actually is? What does your own unexamined assumption about time reveal about how you perceive reality?
?
The author says time's existence is self-evident, yet its reality is obscure. What does that say about the limits of your own understanding? Are you content to merely use time without seeking its true nature?
?
If time were not a real extension but something else—a mere illusion or mental construct—how would that change the way you view your actions, your past, and your future? What would be the spiritual implications for accountability and resurrection?

The First Opinion: Time as a Known Renewed Thing
Presentation and critique of the first philosophical opinion on the reality of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first philosophical opinion on the reality of time and why the author considers it insufficient.

Core Idea
Some say time is a known thing that measures an imaginary renewed thing, but this fails to give a true conception.

Having established that time's existence is self-evident, the author now presents the first attempt to define its reality. This view, attributed to some philosophers, describes time in terms of measurement and relativity. However, as we will see, the author dismisses it as providing no real understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the first opinion
Read the passage describing the first opinion. Notice the terms: 'متجدد معلوم' (known renewed thing) and 'متجدد موهوم' (imaginary renewed thing). Understand the basic claim.
2
Examine the example
The author gives an example of reversal: answering 'When did Zaid stand?' with 'When Amr sat.' This illustrates how time can be relative to the interlocutor's knowledge. Ponder how this example supports the opinion.
The reversal shows that the measurement can vary.
3
Understand the verdict
The author concludes that this does not provide a true conception (إفادة تصور). Reflect on why a definition that relies on relativity might fail to capture the essence of time.
This verdict prepares for the next opinion.

Source Text

Aristotle's Opinion and Its Refutation
Presentation of Aristotle's definition of time and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Aristotle's definition of time as the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere, the arguments supporting it, and the brief refutation offered.

Core Idea
Aristotle defined time as the measure of the outermost sphere's motion, but this view is based on false principles.

After rejecting the first opinion, the author presents the famous Aristotelian definition of time. This view is supported by several arguments, but the author notes that it rests on false principles. In this lesson, we will examine both the arguments and the refutation, seeing how the debate over time's reality reaches a critical juncture.

Guided Path
1
Read Aristotle's definition and its arguments
Read the passage describing Aristotle's view. Identify the definition: 'مقدار حركة الفلك الأعظم' (measure of the motion of the outermost sphere). Then note the five arguments given to support it.
The arguments are dense; take them one by one.
2
Analyze the arguments
The arguments include: variation in quantity, impossibility of composition from instants, instability as measure for motion, impossibility of annihilation, and measurement of all motions by the fastest. Try to understand each argument's logical thread.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation is brief: the view is based on false principles, and would only be valid if time's susceptibility to variation were essential. Consider what this critique implies.
The refutation leaves room for further debate.

?
This passage challenges the very definition of time. Reflect on your own experience of time: do you treat it as something absolute, or as relative to something else? How does this lesson affect how you think about the nature of time and its Creator?

The Ash'ari View of Time and Its Definition Among Theologians
Ash'ari agreement and mutakallimun's definition of time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari consensus on the hiddenness of time's essence and the mutakallimun's pragmatic definition of time as a known measure for unknown changes.

Core Idea
Time's essence is hidden; theologians define it relationally, not as an independent reality.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents the Ash'ari stance on time: while some claimed its 'vessel' is apparent, all agreed its true essence is hidden. The mutakallimun then defined time pragmatically as a known changing thing used to measure unknown changes. The author provides examples to illustrate this relative conception, emphasizing that it does not reveal time's quiddity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two main parts: the Ash'ari consensus on hiddenness and the definition with examples.
The definition is a single sentence: 'It is a known changing thing by which an unknown changing thing is measured.'
2
Identify the definition's components
Break down the definition: time is 'mutajaddid ma'lūm' (known changing thing) used to measure 'mutajaddid ghayr ma'lūm' (unknown changing thing). Look at the examples to see how relativity works.
The examples show that the measure depends on the knowledge of the addressee.
3
Reflect on the limitation
The author concludes that this interpretation does not provide a conception of time's essence. Understand that the definition is pragmatic, not metaphysical.
This aligns with the Ash'ari agreement on hiddenness of essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : فزعمت الأشاعرة (۱) القوم ، وإن ادعى بعضهم ظهور آنية الزمان فقد اتفقوا على خفاء ماهيته . فقال كثير من المتكلمين هو متجدد معلوم مقدر به متجدد غير معلوم كما يقال : آتيك عند طلوع الشمس، وربما يتعاكس بحسب علم المخاطب حتى لو علم وقت قعود عمرو، فقال : متى قام زيد؟ يقال في جوابه : حين قعد عمرو. ولو علم وقت قيام زيد فقال متى قعد عمرو يقال في جوابه حين قام زيد ولذلك يختلف تقدير المتجددات باختلاف ما يعتقد المقدر ظهور عند المخاطب كما تقول العامة للعامة (۲) اجلس يوماً، والقارىء اجلس قدر أما تقر الفاتحة والكاتب قدر ما تكتب صحيفة والتركي قدر ما ينضج مرجل (۳) لحم، ولا يخفى إذ ليس في هذا التفسير إفادة تصور ماهية الزمان
He said: The Ash'aris claimed the people, and even if some of them claimed the appearance of the vessel of time, they agreed on the hiddenness of its essence. So many of the theologians said: It is a known changing thing by which an unknown changing thing is measured, as it is said: 'I will come to you at sunrise,' and perhaps it reverses according to the knowledge of the addressee, such that if one knew the time of 'Amr's sitting and said: 'When did Zayd stand?' it is said in answer: 'When 'Amr sat.' And if one knew the time of Zayd's standing and said: 'When did 'Amr sit?' it is said in answer: 'When Zayd stood.' Therefore, the estimation of changing things differs according to what the one estimating believes appears to the addressee, just as the common people say to the common people: 'Sit for a day,' and the reciter: 'Sit for the measure of your recitation of the Fatiha,' and the writer: 'Sit for the measure of your writing a page,' and the Turk: 'Sit for the measure of a pot of meat cooking.' And it is not hidden that this interpretation does not provide a conception of the essence of time.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage first clarifies the stance of the Ash'ari school on time (az-zaman): although some Ash'aris claimed that the 'vessel' (āniya) of time is apparent, they all agree that its true essence (māhiyya) is hidden. Then it presents the common definition of time among many mutakallimun: time is a known changing thing (mutajaddid ma'lūm) by which an unknown changing thing (mutajaddid ghayr ma'lūm) is measured. The author gives examples to illustrate how relative estimations work depending on the knowledge of the addressee—e.g., answering 'when Zayd stood' with 'when 'Amr sat' if the timing of one is known relative to the other. He then provides further examples from everyday life (a day for common folks, the time to recite the Fatiha for a reciter, etc.) to show that these definitions are pragmatic and do not reveal the true essence of time. The passage concludes by noting that such interpretations do not give a conceptual grasp of time's quiddity.

الماهية

Correction: They define time only as a relative measure, not as an essence; the passage states the interpretation does not give a conception of time's essence.

Time as the Measure of Circular Motion
Al-Taftazani defines time as the measure of circular motion and proves it must be the fastest motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic philosophers define time as the measure of circular motion and how they prove it must be the fastest motion.

Core Idea
Time must measure circular motion because straight motion is discontinuous but time is continuous.

Al-Taftazani presents a rigorous definition of time rooted in Aristotelian physics. He argues that time cannot measure a stationary essence, nor straight motion, but only circular motion. He further establishes that the measure must be the fastest motion, leading to the daily rotation of the celestial sphere.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to get the flow of reasoning.
Notice the logical structure: definition, rejection of alternatives, conclusion.
2
Understand the definition
Note that time is defined as 'measure of a non-stationary essence' which is motion. If it measured a stationary essence, all events would be simultaneous.
3
Follow the argument against straight motion
Why does straight motion fail? Because it is necessarily discontinuous due to finite dimensions, while time is continuous.
This relies on the finitude of the universe's dimensions.
4
See the conclusion
Time must measure circular motion, and specifically the fastest circular motion, the daily rotation of the supreme sphere.
The fastest motion gives the shortest measure, suitable for measuring all motions.
5
Reflect on the significance
This definition connects time to cosmology and motion, grounding it in physical reality as understood by medieval scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنه مقدار لأمر غير قار الذات وهو الحركة وإلا لكان هو أيضاً قار الذات أي مجتمع الأجزاء في الوجود فيكون الحادث في اليوم حادث يوم الطوفان وهو محال ولا يجوز أن يكون مقدار الحركة مستقيمة لأنها لازمة الانقطاع لما سيجيء من تناهي الأبعاد، ومن امتناع اتصال الحركات المستقيمة على مسافة متناهية، والزمان لا ينقطع لما مر فتعين أن يكون مقدار الحركة مستديرة ويلزم أن يكون أسرع الحركات ليكون مقدارها أقصر فتصلح التقدير جميع الحركات به، فإن الأقل يقدر به الأكثر من غير عكس لتقدير الفرسخ بالذراع وتقدير المائة بالعشرة وأسرع الحركات الحركة اليومية المنسوبة إلى الفلك الأعظم فيكون الزمان مقداراً لها.
It is a measure of a non-stationary essence, which is motion, otherwise it would itself be stationary, i.e., having its parts coexistent in existence, so that the event today would be an event on the day of the Flood, which is impossible. And it is not permissible that the measure of motion be straight, because it is necessarily discontinuous due to what will come of the finitude of dimensions, and due to the impossibility of continuity of straight motions over a finite distance, and time does not cease, as has passed, so it is determined that the measure of motion be circular. And it follows that it be the fastest of motions so that its measure be shortest, thus making it suitable for measuring all motions by it. For the lesser is used to measure the greater without the reverse, like measuring a farsakh by a cubit and measuring a hundred by ten. And the fastest of motions is the daily motion attributed to the supreme sphere, so time is a measure of it.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage defines time and argues for its nature. It states that time is the measure of something non-stationary, i.e., motion. If time were stationary, then all events would be simultaneous, which is absurd. Then it argues that the measure cannot be of straight motion because straight motion is necessarily discontinuous due to finite dimensions, while time is continuous. Therefore, it must be of circular motion. Moreover, to measure all motions, it must be the fastest motion, because the smallest measure is used to measure larger ones. The fastest motion is the daily rotation of the outermost sphere, so time is the measure of that motion.

Key Terms
زمان
حركة
مقدار
غير قار الذات
فلك أعظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time could be a measure of straight motion.
Correction: Al-Taftazani argues that straight motion is discontinuous due to finite dimensions, while time is continuous, so time cannot measure straight motion.

Checkpoint

C) Because circular motion is easier to measure.
D) Because all motions are circular.

Key Takeaway
Time is defined as the measure of the fastest circular motion, namely the daily rotation of the celestial sphere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument shows that time cannot be discontinuous—it flows without interruption. Are there parts of your faith that you treat as intermittent, practiced only when convenient? How does this unbroken call to worship challenge you to change?
?
The supreme sphere rotates ceaselessly, and every moment of your life is measured by it. Does your daily schedule reflect the urgency of the Hereafter, or are you lulled into neglecting the speed at which time—and your life—passes?
