Classification of Letters into Consonants and Vowels
Sanusi classifies letters into consonants and vowels and explains short and long vowels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi classifies letters into consonants and vowels, including short and long vowels, the secondary classification into instantaneous and temporal, and the phonetic definition of 'movement'.

Core Idea
Letters are either consonants or vowels; vowels are short (movement) or long (madd).

In this passage, Sanusi provides a foundational phonetic classification of Arabic letters. He begins by quoting the matn that letters divide into consonants and vowels, then explains the two types of vowels—short and long—and introduces another division into instantaneous and temporal. This classification is essential for understanding the structure of Arabic speech and its implications for kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall structure.
Notice the two main divisions: consonant/vowel and instantaneous/temporal.
2
Identify the vowel types
Find where Sanusi splits vowels into short (حركات) and long (مدات). Understand the terms 'مقصور' and 'ممدود'.
Short vowels are the three vowel signs; long vowels are the letters of madd.
3
Understand the definition of 'movement'
Sanusi says 'حركة' here is a quality from inclining the consonant's point of articulation toward a madd letter. Connect this to fatah, dammah, kasrah.
This is not physical motion but a phonetic concept.
4
Examine the impossibility of beginning with a vowel
Read the debate on why starting with a vowel is impossible. Sanusi argues it is due to its essence, not because it is silent.
He proves the possibility of starting with a consonant, relevant to general linguistic capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وينقسم إلى صامت ومصوت مقصور هي الحركات، وممدود هي المدات ومعنى الحركة هاهنا الكيفية الحاصلة من إمالة مخرج الحرف أي مخرج احدى المدات فإلى الواو ضمه وإلى الألف فتحة، وإلى الياء كسرة وامتناع الابتداء بالمصوت لذاته لا لسكونه للقطع بإمكان الابتداء بالساكن وإن لم يجز استعماله في بعض اللغات كالوقف على المتحرك والجمع بين الساكنين من الصامت إلا لقصور في الآلة وينقسم أيضاً إلى أني كالطاء، وزماني كالفاء. الحركات الثلاث تعد عندهم في الحروف وتسمى المصوتة المقصورة والألف والواو والياء إذا كانت ساكنة متولدة من حركات تجانسها أعني الألف من الفتحة والواو من الضمة، والياء من الكسرة تسمى المصوتة المسماة في العربية بحروف المد لأنها كانت مدات للحركات، الممدودة، وهي وما سوى المصوتة تسمى صامتة، ويندرج فيها الواو والياء المتحركتان أو الساكنتان، إذ لم يكن قبل الواو ضمة وقبل الياء كسرة وأما الألف فلا يكون إلا مصوتاً، وإطلاقها على الهمزة باشتراك الاسم، وليس المراد بالحركة والسكون هاهنا ما هو من خواص الأجسام، بل الحركة عبارة عن كيفية حاصلة في الحرف الصامت من إمالة مخرجه إلى مخرج إحدى المدات، فإلى الألف فتحة وإلى الواو ضمة. وإلى الياء كسرة. ولا خلاف في امتناع الابتداء بالمصوت وإنما الخلاف في أن ذلك بسكونه حتى يمتنع الابتداء بالساكن الصامت أيضاً أو لذاته لكونه عبارة عن مدة متولدة من إشباع حركة تجانسها فلا يتصور إلا حيث يكون قبلها صامت متحرك وهذا هو الحق لأن كل سليم الحس يجد من نفسه إمكان الابتداء بالساكن، وإن كان مرفوضاً في لغة العرب كالوقف على المتحرك والجمع بين الساكنين من الصوامت إلا في الوقف مثل زيد وعمرو، أو إذا كان الصامت الأول حرف لين والثاني مدغماً نحو خويصة فإنه جائز، كما إذا كان الأول مصوتاً نحو دابة وعدم قدرة البعض على الابتداء بالساكن لا يدل على امتناعه كالتلفظ ببعض الحروف فإن ذلك لقصور في الآلة والاستدلال على الإمكان بأن المصوت أينما كان مشروط بالصامت، فلو كان الصامت مشروطاً به في بعض المواضع كالابتداء لزم الدور، وليس بشيء لأن المصوت مشروط بأن يسبقه صامت والصامت في الابتداء مشروط بأن يلحقه مصوت فيكونان معاً ولا إستحالة فيه . قوله : وينقسم أي الحرف باعتبار آخر إلى أني وزماني لأنه إن أمكن
He said: It is divided into consonant and vowel: the short (vowels) are the movements, and the long are the madds. The meaning of movement here is the quality resulting from inclining the point of articulation of the letter—i.e., the point of articulation of one of the madds: towards the waw is dammah, towards the alif is fatah, and towards the ya is kasrah. And the impossibility of beginning with a vowel is due to its essence, not due to its being silent, because it is established that beginning with a consonant is possible, even if it is not used in some languages, like pausing on a vowel and combining two consonants, except due to a deficiency in the speech organ. It is also divided into instantaneous, like ṭāʾ, and temporal, like fāʾ. The three vowel sounds are considered by them as letters and are called the short vowels. And alif, waw, and yā when they are silent and generated from their homorganic vowels—i.e., alif from fatah, waw from dammah, and yā from kasrah—are called the vowels, named in Arabic 'letters of madd' because they are extensions of the vowels. And other than the vowel is called consonant, and includes waw and yā when they are vowelled or silent if there is not before them a dammah (before waw) or a kasrah (before yā). As for alif, it is only a vowel, and applying the term to the hamza is by homonymy. What is meant by movement and silence here is not what belongs to the properties of bodies; rather, movement is a quality that occurs in the consonant letter from inclining its point of articulation to the point of articulation of one of the madds: to alif is fatah, to waw is dammah, and to yā is kasrah. There is no disagreement about the impossibility of beginning with a vowel, but the disagreement is whether that is due to its being silent so that beginning with a silent consonant is also impossible, or due to its essence because it is an extension generated from the fullness of a homorganic vowel, so it can only be conceived where there is a preceding vowelled consonant. This is the truth, because every sound person finds in himself the possibility of beginning with a consonant, even if it is rejected in the Arabic language, like pausing on a vowel and combining two consonants, except in pausing like Zayd and ʿAmr, or if the first consonant is a soft letter and the second is assimilated like khawayṣah, then it is permissible, just as if the first is a vowel like dābbah. The inability of some to begin with a consonant does not prove its impossibility, like pronouncing some letters; that is due to a deficiency in the speech organ. The inference for possibility that the vowel wherever it is is conditioned by a consonant, so if the consonant were conditioned by it in some places like the beginning, a circularity would arise, is not valid, because the vowel is conditioned by a consonant preceding it, and the consonant at the beginning is conditioned by a vowel following it, so they occur together and there is no impossibility in that. He said: It is divided—i.e., the letter, from another consideration—into instantaneous and temporal, because if it is possible...
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of letters. The author (Sanusi) begins by quoting the matn: letters are divided into consonant (صامت) and vowel (مصوت). The vowels are further divided into short (مقصور) which are the three vowel signs (حركات), and long (ممدود) which are the madd letters. He then explains that 'movement' (حركة) here does not mean physical motion but a quality resulting from inclining the point of articulation of a consonant towards the point of articulation of one of the madd letters: towards waw gives dammah, towards alif gives fatah, towards ya gives kasrah. He then mentions the impossibility of beginning a word with a vowel (مصوت) and says it is due to its essence, not because it is silent (ساكن), since beginning with a consonant is possible even if not used in some languages. Letters are also divided into instantaneous (آني) like ṭāʾ and temporal (زماني) like fāʾ. Next, he explains that the three short vowels are considered letters and are called 'short vowels' (المصوتة المقصورة). The alif, waw, and ya when silent and generated from homorganic vowels are called 'vowels' and in Arabic 'letters of madd' because they extend the vowels. Everything other than these vowels is called consonant, including waw and ya when vowelled or silent if not preceded by the appropriate vowel; alif is always a vowel, and calling the hamza 'alif' is just homonymy. He then clarifies that the terms 'movement' and 'silence' here are not the physical properties of bodies; movement is the quality that occurs in the consonant from inclining its point of articulation to the point of articulation of a madd letter. He then discusses the disagreement about why beginning with a vowel is impossible: some say it is because it is silent (like a silent consonant), but the truth is that it is impossible due to its essence, because a vowel is an extension that only occurs after a vowelled consonant. He argues that beginning with a consonant is possible, even if not used in Arabic, and gives examples. He refutes an objection that would lead to circularity, showing that the conditions of vowel following consonant and consonant preceding vowel are different and can coexist. Finally, he comments on the classification into instantaneous and temporal, noting that this is from another consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The terms 'movement' and 'silence' in this context refer to physical motion and stillness of bodies.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that here they refer to qualities of articulation: movement is the quality resulting from inclining the point of articulation of a consonant towards a madd letter, and silence is the absence of that.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi say beginning with a vowel (مصوت) is impossible?
A) Because it is silent
B) Due to its essence, because it is an extension that requires a preceding vowelled consonant
C) Because Arabic grammar forbids it
D) Because all languages prohibit it

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's classification of letters into consonants and vowels, with further subdivisions, provides a precise phonetic framework for analyzing the Arabic language's structure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about Allah's attributes, how often do you settle for a shallow understanding that might be based on a misconception, like the one about 'movement' being physical? How will you ensure you seek the precise meaning as intended by scholars, even if it requires effort?
?
This lesson shows that even something as basic as letters has a precise classification with far-reaching implications. How does this challenge your own approach to learning about your faith? Are you willing to dig deeper into the details, or do you prefer to stay on the surface?
?
Sanusi refutes a wrong opinion by careful reasoning, not by mere assertion. Think of an area of your belief where you might have assumed something without proper evidence. How can you apply the same analytical approach to ensure your aqeedah is sound, and what practical step will you take this week to verify one such assumption?
